						<div id="siteNotice"><!-- CentralNotice --></div>
						<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en"><span dir="auto">Diesel engine</span></h1>
			<div id="bodyContent">
								<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-navigation">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Diesel_generator_on_an_oil_tanker.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Diesel_generator_on_an_oil_tanker.jpg/220px-Diesel_generator_on_an_oil_tanker.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Diesel_generator_on_an_oil_tanker.jpg/330px-Diesel_generator_on_an_oil_tanker.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Diesel_generator_on_an_oil_tanker.jpg/440px-Diesel_generator_on_an_oil_tanker.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Diesel_generator_on_an_oil_tanker.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Diesel generator on an oil tanker</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dieselmotor_vs.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Dieselmotor_vs.jpg/220px-Dieselmotor_vs.jpg" width="220" height="313" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Dieselmotor_vs.jpg/330px-Dieselmotor_vs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Dieselmotor_vs.jpg/440px-Dieselmotor_vs.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dieselmotor_vs.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
A Diesel engine built by <a href="/wiki/MAN_SE" title="MAN SE">MAN AG</a> in 1906</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <b>diesel engine</b> (also known as a <b>compression-ignition engine</b>) is an <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engine</a> that uses the <a href="/wiki/Heat_of_compression" title="Heat of compression" class="mw-redirect">heat of compression</a> to initiate <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">ignition</a> to burn the <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">fuel</a> that has been injected into the <a href="/wiki/Combustion_chamber" title="Combustion chamber">combustion chamber</a>. As opposed to spark-ignition engines such as a <a href="/wiki/Petrol_engine" title="Petrol engine">petrol engine</a> (gasoline engine) or <a href="/wiki/Gas_engine" title="Gas engine">gas engine</a> (using a gaseous fuel as opposed to <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a>), which uses a <a href="/wiki/Spark_plug" title="Spark plug">spark plug</a> to ignite an air-fuel mixture. The engine was developed by German inventor <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Diesel" title="Rudolf Diesel">Rudolf Diesel</a> in 1893.</p>
<p>The diesel engine has the highest <a href="/wiki/Thermal_efficiency" title="Thermal efficiency">thermal efficiency</a> of any standard <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion" title="Internal combustion" class="mw-redirect">internal</a> or <a href="/wiki/External_combustion" title="External combustion" class="mw-redirect">external combustion</a> engine due to its very high <a href="/wiki/Compression_ratio" title="Compression ratio">compression ratio</a>. Low-speed diesel engines (as used in ships and other applications where overall engine weight is relatively unimportant) can have a thermal efficiency that exceeds 50%.<sup id="cite_ref-bmmWW_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bmmWW-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mhi_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mhi-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Diesel engines are manufactured in <a href="/wiki/Two-stroke" title="Two-stroke" class="mw-redirect">two-stroke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Four-stroke" title="Four-stroke" class="mw-redirect">four-stroke</a> versions. They were originally used as a more efficient replacement for stationary <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engines</a>. Since the 1910s they have been used in <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarines</a> and ships. Use in locomotives, trucks, <a href="/wiki/Heavy_equipment" title="Heavy equipment">heavy equipment</a> and electric generating plants followed later. In the 1930s, they slowly began to be used in a few <a href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile">automobiles</a>. Since the 1970s, the use of diesel engines in larger on-road and <a href="/wiki/Off-road_vehicle" title="Off-road vehicle">off-road vehicles</a> in the USA increased. According to the British Society of Motor Manufacturing and Traders, the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">EU</a> average for diesel cars account for 50% of the total sold, including 70% in France and 38% in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The world's largest diesel engine is currently a <a href="/wiki/W%C3%A4rtsil%C3%A4-Sulzer_RTA96-C" title="Wärtsilä-Sulzer RTA96-C">Wärtsilä-Sulzer RTA96-C</a> Common Rail marine diesel of about 84,420&#160;kW (113,210&#160;hp) @ 102&#160;rpm<sup id="cite_ref-WSRT96C_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSRT96C-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> output.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-49"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a>
<ul>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-50"><a href="#Patents"><span class="tocnumber">14.1</span> <span class="toctext">Patents</span></a></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Diesel" title="Rudolf Diesel">Rudolf Diesel</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Diesel%27s_Engine.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Diesel%27s_Engine.jpg/220px-Diesel%27s_Engine.jpg" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Diesel%27s_Engine.jpg/330px-Diesel%27s_Engine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Diesel%27s_Engine.jpg/440px-Diesel%27s_Engine.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Diesel%27s_Engine.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Diesel's original 1897 engine on display at the Deutsches Museum in Munich, Germany</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Diesel" title="Rudolf Diesel">Rudolf Diesel</a> was born in Paris in 1858 into a family of German expatriates.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> He was educated at <a href="/wiki/Technical_University_of_Munich" title="Technical University of Munich" class="mw-redirect">Munich Polytechnic</a>. After graduation he was employed as a refrigerator engineer, but his true love lay in engine design. Diesel designed many heat engines, including a solar-powered air engine. In 1892 he received patents in Germany, Switzerland, the United Kingdom and filed in the United States for "Method of and Apparatus for Converting Heat into Work".<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1893 he described a "slow-combustion engine" that first compressed air thereby raising its temperature above the igniting-point of the fuel, then gradually introducing fuel while letting the mixture expand "against resistance sufficiently to prevent an essential increase of temperature and pressure", then cutting off fuel and "expanding without transfer of heat".<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In 1894 and 1895 he filed patents and addenda in various countries for his Diesel engine; the first patents were issued in Spain (No.&#160;16,654), France (No.&#160;243,531) and Belgium (No.&#160;113,139) in December 1894, and in Germany (No.&#160;86,633) in 1895 and the United States (No.&#160;608,845) in 1898.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> He operated his first successful engine in 1897.</p>
<p>Though best known for his invention of the pressure-ignited heat engine that bears his name, Rudolf Diesel was also a well-respected thermal engineer and a social theorist. Diesel's inventions have three points in common: they relate to heat transfer by natural physical processes or laws; they involve markedly creative mechanical design; and they were initially motivated by the inventor's concept of <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">sociological</a> needs. Rudolf Diesel originally conceived the diesel engine to enable independent craftsmen and artisans to compete with <a href="/wiki/Industry" title="Industry">industry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At <a href="/wiki/Augsburg" title="Augsburg">Augsburg</a>, on August 10, 1893, Rudolf Diesel's prime model, a single 10-foot (3.0&#160;m) iron cylinder with a <a href="/wiki/Flywheel" title="Flywheel">flywheel</a> at its base, ran on its own power for the first time. Diesel spent two more years making improvements and in 1896 demonstrated another model with a theoretical efficiency of 75%, in contrast to the 10% efficiency of the <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engine</a>. By 1898, Diesel had become a millionaire. His engines were used to power <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">pipelines</a>, electric and water plants, automobiles and trucks, and marine craft. They were soon to be used in mines, <a href="/wiki/Oil_field" title="Oil field">oil fields</a>, factories, and transoceanic shipping.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_timeline">History timeline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History timeline">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History timeline" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" style="" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width: 52px;"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" /></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text" style=""><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for verification</b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be <a href="/wiki/Template:Citation_needed" title="Template:Citation needed">challenged</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <small><i>(April 2010)</i></small> </span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_internal_combustion_engine" title="History of the internal combustion engine">History of the internal combustion engine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rudolf_Diesel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Rudolf_Diesel.jpg/220px-Rudolf_Diesel.jpg" width="220" height="252" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/39/Rudolf_Diesel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/39/Rudolf_Diesel.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rudolf_Diesel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Rudolf Diesel</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>1890s</p>
<ul>
<li>1892: February 23, <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Diesel" title="Rudolf Diesel">Rudolf Diesel</a> obtained a patent (RP 67207) titled "<i>Arbeitsverfahren und Ausführungsart für Verbrennungsmaschinen</i>".</li>
<li>1893: Diesel's essay titled <i>Theory and Construction of a Rational Heat-engine to Replace the Steam Engine and Combustion Engines Known Today</i> appeared.</li>
<li>1893: August 10, Diesel built his first working prototype in Augsburg.<sup id="cite_ref-Busch_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Busch-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>1897: Adolphus Busch licenses rights to the Diesel Engine for the USA and Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-Busch_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Busch-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>1898 Diesel licensed his engine to <a href="/wiki/Branobel" title="Branobel">Branobel</a>, a Russian oil company interested in an engine that could consume non-distilled oil. Branobel's engineers spent four years designing a ship-mounted engine.</li>
<li>1899: Diesel licensed his engine to builders <a href="/wiki/Krupp" title="Krupp">Krupp</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulzer_(manufacturer)" title="Sulzer (manufacturer)">Sulzer</a>, who quickly became major manufacturers.</li>
</ul>
<p>1900s</p>
<ul>
<li>1902: Until 1910 <a href="/wiki/MAN_SE" title="MAN SE">MAN</a> produced 82 copies of the stationary diesel engine.</li>
<li>1903: Two first diesel-powered ships were launched, both for river and canal operations: <i>Petite-Pierre</i> in France, powered by Dyckhoff-built diesels, and <i><a href="/wiki/Vandal_(tanker)" title="Vandal (tanker)">Vandal</a></i> tanker in Russia, powered by Swedish-built diesels with an electrical transmission.</li>
<li>1904: The French built the first diesel <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarine</a>, the Z.</li>
<li>1905: Four diesel engine <a href="/wiki/Turbocharger" title="Turbocharger">turbochargers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Intercooler" title="Intercooler">intercoolers</a> were manufactured by Büchl (CH), as well as a <a href="/wiki/Scroll-type_supercharger" title="Scroll-type supercharger">scroll-type supercharger</a> from Creux (F) company.</li>
<li>1908: <a href="/wiki/Prosper_L%27Orange" title="Prosper L'Orange">Prosper L'Orange</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deutz_AG" title="Deutz AG">Deutz</a> developed a precisely controlled injection pump with a needle injection nozzle.</li>
<li>1909: The prechamber with a hemispherical combustion chamber was developed by Prosper L'Orange with <a href="/wiki/Benz_%26_Cie." title="Benz &amp; Cie." class="mw-redirect">Benz</a>.</li>
</ul>
<p>1910s</p>
<ul>
<li>1910: The Norwegian research ship <i><a href="/wiki/Fram" title="Fram">Fram</a></i> was a sailing ship fitted with an auxiliary diesel engine, and was thus the first ocean-going ship with a diesel engine.<sup id="cite_ref-vulcanus.oli.tudelft.nl_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vulcanus.oli.tudelft.nl-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>1912: The Danish built the first ocean-going ship exclusively powered by a diesel engine, MS <i><a href="/wiki/Selandia" title="Selandia" class="mw-redirect">Selandia</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-vulcanus.oli.tudelft.nl_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vulcanus.oli.tudelft.nl-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> The first <a href="/wiki/Locomotive" title="Locomotive">locomotive</a> with a diesel engine also appeared.</li>
<li>1913: <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">U.S. Navy</a> submarines used <a href="/wiki/New_London_Ship_and_Engine_Company" title="New London Ship and Engine Company">NELSECO</a> units. <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Diesel" title="Rudolf Diesel">Rudolf Diesel</a> died mysteriously when he crossed the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a> on the <a href="/wiki/SS_Dresden_(1897)" title="SS Dresden (1897)">SS <i>Dresden</i></a>.</li>
<li>1914: German <a href="/wiki/U-boat" title="U-boat">U-boats</a> were powered by MAN diesels.</li>
<li>1919: Prosper L'Orange obtained a patent on a prechamber insert and made a needle injection nozzle. First diesel engine from <a href="/wiki/Cummins" title="Cummins">Cummins</a>.</li>
</ul>
<p>1920s</p>
<ul>
<li>1921: Prosper L'Orange built a continuous variable output <a href="/wiki/Injection_pump" title="Injection pump">injection pump</a>.</li>
<li>1922: The first vehicle with a (pre-chamber) diesel engine was <a href="/wiki/Tractor" title="Tractor">Agricultural Tractor</a> Type 6 of the <a href="/wiki/Karl_Benz#Toward_Daimler-Benz_and_the_first_Mercedes-Benz_in_1926" title="Karl Benz">Benz Söhne</a> agricultural tractor OE Benz Sendling.</li>
<li>1923: The first <a href="/wiki/Truck" title="Truck">truck</a> with pre-chamber diesel engine made by <a href="/wiki/MAN_SE" title="MAN SE">MAN</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benz_%26_Cie." title="Benz &amp; Cie." class="mw-redirect">Benz</a>. <a href="/wiki/Daimler-Motoren-Gesellschaft" title="Daimler-Motoren-Gesellschaft">Daimler-Motoren-Gesellschaft</a> testing the first air-injection diesel-engined truck.</li>
<li>1924: The introduction on the truck market of the diesel engine by commercial truck manufacturers in the IAA. <a href="/wiki/Fairbanks-Morse" title="Fairbanks-Morse">Fairbanks-Morse</a> starts building diesel engines.</li>
<li>1927: First <a href="/wiki/Truck" title="Truck">truck</a> injection pump and injection nozzles of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bosch_GmbH" title="Robert Bosch GmbH">Bosch</a>. First passenger car prototype of <a href="/wiki/Stoewer" title="Stoewer">Stoewer</a>.</li>
</ul>
<p>1930s</p>
<ul>
<li>1930s: <a href="/wiki/Caterpillar_Inc." title="Caterpillar Inc.">Caterpillar</a> started building diesels for their tractors.</li>
<li>1930: First US diesel-power passenger car (<a href="/wiki/Cummins" title="Cummins">Cummins</a> powered Packard) built in Columbus, Indiana (USA)<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>1930: <a href="/wiki/Beardmore_Tornado" title="Beardmore Tornado">Beardmore Tornado</a> diesel engines power the British airship <a href="/wiki/R101" title="R101">R101</a></li>
<li>1932: Introduction of the strongest diesel truck in the world by MAN with 160&#160;hp (120&#160;kW).</li>
<li>1933: First European <a href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile">passenger cars</a> with diesel engines (<a href="/wiki/Citro%C3%ABn" title="Citroën">Citroën</a> Rosalie); Citroën used an engine of the English diesel pioneer Sir <a href="/wiki/Harry_Ricardo" title="Harry Ricardo">Harry Ricardo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> The car did not go into production due to legal restrictions on the use of diesel engines.</li>
<li>1934: First <a href="/wiki/Turbo" title="Turbo" class="mw-redirect">turbo</a> diesel engine for a <a href="/wiki/Railway_train" title="Railway train" class="mw-redirect">railway train</a> by <a href="/wiki/Maybach" title="Maybach">Maybach</a>. First streamlined, stainless steel passenger train in the US, the <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_Zephyr" title="Pioneer Zephyr">Pioneer Zephyr</a>, using a Winton engine.</li>
<li>1934: First tank equipped with diesel engine, the Polish <a href="/wiki/7TP" title="7TP">7TP</a>.</li>
<li>1934–35: <a href="/wiki/Junkers_(Aircraft)" title="Junkers (Aircraft)" class="mw-redirect">Junkers Motorenwerke</a> in Germany started production of the Jumo aviation diesel engine family, the most famous of these being the <a href="/wiki/Junkers_Jumo_205" title="Junkers Jumo 205">Jumo 205</a>, of which over 900 examples were produced by the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lumbar_patent_dieselengine.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Lumbar_patent_dieselengine.jpg/220px-Lumbar_patent_dieselengine.jpg" width="220" height="333" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Lumbar_patent_dieselengine.jpg/330px-Lumbar_patent_dieselengine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Lumbar_patent_dieselengine.jpg/440px-Lumbar_patent_dieselengine.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lumbar_patent_dieselengine.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Diesel" title="Rudolf Diesel">Rudolf Diesel</a>'s 1893 patent on his engine design</div>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li>1936: <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz" title="Mercedes-Benz">Mercedes-Benz</a> built the <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz_260_D" title="Mercedes-Benz 260 D">260D</a> diesel car.<sup id="cite_ref-MB260D_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MB260D-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Atchison,_Topeka_and_Santa_Fe_Railway" title="Atchison, Topeka and Santa Fe Railway">AT&amp;SF</a> inaugurated the diesel train <a href="/wiki/Super_Chief" title="Super Chief">Super Chief</a>. The airship <a href="/wiki/Hindenburg_(airship)" title="Hindenburg (airship)" class="mw-redirect">Hindenburg</a> was powered by diesel engines. First series of passenger cars manufactured with diesel engine (<a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz" title="Mercedes-Benz">Mercedes-Benz</a> 260 D, <a href="/wiki/Hanomag" title="Hanomag">Hanomag</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saurer" title="Saurer">Saurer</a>). <a href="/wiki/Daimler_Benz" title="Daimler Benz" class="mw-redirect">Daimler Benz</a> airship diesel engine 602LOF6 for the LZ129 <i>Hindenburg</i> <a href="/wiki/Airship" title="Airship">airship</a>.</li>
<li>1937: The <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> chose a diesel engine for its <a href="/wiki/T-34" title="T-34">T-34</a> tank, widely regarded as the best tank chassis of World War II.</li>
<li>1937: <a href="/wiki/BMW_114" title="BMW 114">BMW 114</a> experimental airplane diesel engine development.</li>
<li>1938: First turbo diesel engine of <a href="/wiki/Saurer" title="Saurer">Saurer</a>.</li>
</ul>
<p>1940s</p>
<ul>
<li>1942: <a href="/wiki/Tatra_(company)" title="Tatra (company)">Tatra</a> started production of <a href="/wiki/Tatra_111" title="Tatra 111">Tatra 111</a> with <a href="/wiki/Air_cooling" title="Air cooling">air-cooled</a> V12 diesel engine.</li>
<li>1943-'46: The Common-rail (CRD) system was invented (and patented by) Clessie Cummins<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>1944: Development of <a href="/wiki/Air_cooling" title="Air cooling">air cooling</a> for diesel engines by <a href="/wiki/Deutz_AG" title="Deutz AG">Klöckner Humboldt Deutz AG</a> (KHD) for the production stage, and later also for Magirus Deutz.</li>
</ul>
<p>1950s</p>
<ul>
<li>1953: <a href="/wiki/Turbocharger" title="Turbocharger">Turbo</a>-diesel truck for <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz" title="Mercedes-Benz">Mercedes</a> in small series.</li>
<li>1954: Turbo-diesel truck in mass production by <a href="/wiki/Volvo" title="Volvo">Volvo</a>. First diesel engine with an overhead <a href="/wiki/Cam_shaft" title="Cam shaft" class="mw-redirect">cam shaft</a> of <a href="/wiki/Daimler_Benz" title="Daimler Benz" class="mw-redirect">Daimler Benz</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>1960s</p>
<ul>
<li>1960: The diesel drive displaced <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbines</a> and coal fired steam engines.</li>
<li>1962-'65: A <a href="/wiki/Compression_release_engine_brake" title="Compression release engine brake">diesel compression braking system</a>, eventually to be manufactured by Jacobs (of drill chuck fame) and nicknamed the "Jake Brake", was invented and patented by Clessie Cummins.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>1968: <a href="/wiki/Peugeot" title="Peugeot">Peugeot</a> introduced the first <a href="/wiki/Peugeot_204" title="Peugeot 204">204</a> small cars with a transversally mounted diesel engine and <a href="/wiki/Front-wheel_drive" title="Front-wheel drive">front-wheel drive</a>.</li>
</ul>
<p>1970s</p>
<ul>
<li>1973: <a href="/wiki/DAF_Trucks" title="DAF Trucks">DAF</a> produced an air-cooled diesel engine.</li>
<li>1976 February: Tested a diesel engine for the <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen" title="Volkswagen">Volkswagen</a> Golf passenger car. The Cummins <a href="/wiki/Common_Rail" title="Common Rail" class="mw-redirect">Common Rail</a> injection system was further developed by the ETH Zurich from 1976 to 1992.</li>
<li>1978: Mercedes produced the first passenger car turbo-diesels (Mercedes 300 SD).<sup id="cite_ref-MB260D_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MB260D-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>1978: <a href="/wiki/Oldsmobile_Diesel_engine" title="Oldsmobile Diesel engine">Oldsmobile</a> introduced the first passenger car diesel engine produced by an American car company.</li>
</ul>
<p>1980s</p>
<ul>
<li>1985: ATI Intercooler diesel engine from DAF. European Truck Common Rail system with the IFA truck type W50 introduced.</li>
<li>1986: <a href="/wiki/Electronic_Diesel_Control" title="Electronic Diesel Control">Electronic Diesel Control</a> (EDC) of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bosch_GmbH" title="Robert Bosch GmbH">Bosch</a> with the <a href="/wiki/BMW" title="BMW">BMW</a> 524tD.</li>
<li>1986: The <a href="/wiki/Fiat_Croma" title="Fiat Croma">Fiat Croma</a> was the first passenger car in the world to have a <a href="/wiki/Diesel_engine#Direct_injection" title="Diesel engine">direct injection</a> turbodiesel engine in (1986).<sup id="cite_ref-fiat.com_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fiat.com-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>1987: Most powerful production truck with a 460&#160;hp (340&#160;kW) MAN diesel engine.</li>
</ul>
<p>1990s</p>
<ul>
<li>1991: European emission standards <a href="/wiki/Euro_1" title="Euro 1" class="mw-redirect">Euro 1</a> met with the truck diesel engine of <a href="/wiki/Scania_AB" title="Scania AB">Scania</a>.</li>
<li>1993: Pump nozzle injection introduced in Volvo truck engines.</li>
<li>1994: Unit injector system by Bosch for diesel engines.</li>
<li>1995: First successful use of <a href="/wiki/Common_rail" title="Common rail">common rail</a> in a production vehicle, by <a href="/wiki/Denso" title="Denso">Denso</a> in Japan, <a href="/wiki/Hino_Motors" title="Hino Motors">Hino</a> "Rising Ranger" truck.</li>
<li>1997: First <a href="/wiki/Common_rail" title="Common rail">common rail</a> in passenger car, <a href="/wiki/Alfa_Romeo_156" title="Alfa Romeo 156">Alfa Romeo 156</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autonews.com_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autonews.com-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>1998: BMW made history by winning the 24 Hour <a href="/wiki/N%C3%BCrburgring" title="Nürburgring">Nürburgring</a> race with the 320d, powered by a two-litre, four-cylinder diesel engine. The combination of high-performance with better fuel efficiency allowed the team to make fewer pit stops during the long endurance race.</li>
<li>1999: <a href="/wiki/Euro_3" title="Euro 3" class="mw-redirect">Euro 3</a> of Scania and the first <a href="/wiki/Common_rail" title="Common rail">common rail</a> truck diesel engine of <a href="/wiki/Renault" title="Renault">Renault</a>.</li>
</ul>
<p>2000s</p>
<ul>
<li>2002: A street-driven Dodge Dakota pickup with a 735 horsepower (548&#160;kW) diesel engine built at <a href="/wiki/Gale_banks_engineering" title="Gale banks engineering" class="mw-redirect">Gale banks engineering</a> hauls its own service trailer to the <a href="/wiki/Bonneville_Salt_Flats" title="Bonneville Salt Flats">Bonneville Salt Flats</a> and set an <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9d%C3%A9ration_Internationale_de_l%27Automobile" title="Fédération Internationale de l'Automobile">FIA</a> land speed record as the world's fastest pickup truck with a one-way run of 222&#160;mph (357&#160;km/h) and a two-way average of 217&#160;mph (349&#160;km/h).</li>
<li>2004: In Western Europe, the proportion of passenger cars with diesel engine exceeded 50%. Selective catalytic reduction (SCR) system in Mercedes, Euro 4 with EGR system and particle filters of <a href="/wiki/MAN_SE" title="MAN SE">MAN</a>. <a href="/wiki/Piezoelectric" title="Piezoelectric" class="mw-redirect">Piezoelectric</a> injector technology by Bosch.</li>
<li>2006: <a href="/wiki/Audi_R10_TDI" title="Audi R10 TDI">Audi R10 TDI</a> won 12 hours running in Sebring and defeated all other engine concepts. The same car won the <a href="/wiki/2006_24_Hours_of_Le_Mans" title="2006 24 Hours of Le Mans">2006 24 Hours of Le Mans</a>. <a href="/wiki/Euro_5" title="Euro 5" class="mw-redirect">Euro 5</a> for all <a href="/wiki/Iveco" title="Iveco">Iveco</a> trucks.</li>
<li>2006: <a href="/wiki/JCB_Dieselmax" title="JCB Dieselmax">JCB Dieselmax</a> broke the FIA Diesel Land speed record from 1973, eventually setting the new record at over 350&#160;mph (563&#160;km/h).</li>
<li>2008: <a href="/wiki/Subaru" title="Subaru">Subaru</a> introduced the first <a href="/wiki/Horizontally_opposed" title="Horizontally opposed" class="mw-redirect">horizontally opposed</a> diesel engine to be fitted to a passenger car. This is a Euro 5 compliant engine with an <a href="/wiki/Exhaust_gas_recirculation" title="Exhaust gas recirculation">EGR</a> system.</li>
<li>2009: <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen" title="Volkswagen">Volkswagen</a> won the 2009 Dakar Rally held in Argentina and Chile. The first diesel to do so. Race Touareg 2 finished 1st and 2nd.</li>
<li>2009: <a href="/wiki/Volvo" title="Volvo">Volvo</a> claimed the world's strongest truck with their FH16 700. An inline 6-cylinder, 16&#160;L (976&#160;cu&#160;in) 700&#160;hp (522&#160;kW) diesel engine producing 3150 Nm (2323.32&#160;lb•ft) of torque and fully complying with Euro 5 emission standards.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>2010s</p>
<ul>
<li>2010: <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_Motors" title="Mitsubishi Motors">Mitsubishi</a> developed and started mass production of its <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_4N1_engine" title="Mitsubishi 4N1 engine">4N13</a> 1.8&#160;L DOHC I4, the world's first passenger car diesel engine that features a <a href="/wiki/Variable_valve_timing" title="Variable valve timing">variable valve timing</a> system.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>2010: <a href="/wiki/Scania_AB" title="Scania AB">Scania AB</a>'s V8 had the highest torque and power ratings of any truck engine: 730&#160;hp (544&#160;kW) and 3,500&#160;N·m (2,581&#160;ft·lb).<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="How_diesel_engines_work">How diesel engines work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: How diesel engines work">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: How diesel engines work" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DieselCycle_PV.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/DieselCycle_PV.svg/220px-DieselCycle_PV.svg.png" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/DieselCycle_PV.svg/330px-DieselCycle_PV.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/DieselCycle_PV.svg/440px-DieselCycle_PV.svg.png 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DieselCycle_PV.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
p-V Diagram for the Ideal Diesel cycle. The cycle follows the numbers 1-4 in clockwise direction. In the diesel cycle the combustion occurs at almost constant pressure and the exhaust occurs at constant volume. On this diagram the work that is generated for each cycle corresponds to the area within the loop.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Model_Engine_Luc_Viatour.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Model_Engine_Luc_Viatour.jpg/220px-Model_Engine_Luc_Viatour.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Model_Engine_Luc_Viatour.jpg/330px-Model_Engine_Luc_Viatour.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Model_Engine_Luc_Viatour.jpg/440px-Model_Engine_Luc_Viatour.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Model_Engine_Luc_Viatour.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Diesel engine model, left side</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Model_Engine_B_Luc_Viatour.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Model_Engine_B_Luc_Viatour.jpg/220px-Model_Engine_B_Luc_Viatour.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Model_Engine_B_Luc_Viatour.jpg/330px-Model_Engine_B_Luc_Viatour.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Model_Engine_B_Luc_Viatour.jpg/440px-Model_Engine_B_Luc_Viatour.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Model_Engine_B_Luc_Viatour.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Diesel engine model, right side</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="rellink boilerplate seealso">See also: <a href="/wiki/Diesel_cycle" title="Diesel cycle">Diesel cycle</a></div>
<p>The diesel internal combustion engine differs from the gasoline powered Otto cycle by using highly compressed hot air to ignite the fuel rather than using a spark plug (<i>compression ignition</i> rather than <i>spark ignition</i>).</p>
<p>In the true diesel engine, only air is initially introduced into the combustion chamber. The air is then compressed with a compression ratio typically between 15:1 and 22:1 resulting in 40-bar (4.0&#160;MPa; 580&#160;psi) pressure compared to 8 to 14 bars (0.80 to 1.4 MPa; 120 to 200 psi) in the petrol engine. This high compression heats the air to <span style="white-space:nowrap;">550 °C</span> <span style="white-space:nowrap;">(1,022&#160;°F)</span>. At about the top of the compression stroke, fuel is injected directly into the compressed air in the combustion chamber. This may be into a (typically toroidal) void in the top of the piston or a <i>pre-chamber</i> depending upon the design of the engine. The fuel injector ensures that the fuel is broken down into small droplets, and that the fuel is distributed evenly. The heat of the compressed air vaporizes fuel from the surface of the droplets. The vapour is then ignited by the heat from the compressed air in the combustion chamber, the droplets continue to vaporise from their surfaces and burn, getting smaller, until all the fuel in the droplets has been burnt. The start of vaporisation causes a delay period during ignition and the characteristic diesel knocking sound as the vapour reaches ignition temperature and causes an abrupt increase in pressure above the piston. The rapid expansion of combustion gases then drives the piston downward, supplying power to the crankshaft.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As well as the high level of compression allowing combustion to take place without a separate ignition system, a high <a href="/wiki/Compression_ratio" title="Compression ratio">compression ratio</a> greatly increases the engine's efficiency. Increasing the compression ratio in a spark-ignition engine where fuel and air are mixed before entry to the cylinder is limited by the need to prevent damaging <a href="/wiki/Pre-ignition#Pre-ignition" title="Pre-ignition" class="mw-redirect">pre-ignition</a>. Since only air is compressed in a diesel engine, and fuel is not introduced into the cylinder until shortly before top dead centre (<a href="/wiki/Top_Dead_Center" title="Top Dead Center" class="mw-redirect">TDC</a>), premature detonation is not an issue and compression ratios are much higher.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_fuel_injection_systems">Early fuel injection systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early fuel injection systems">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early fuel injection systems" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Diesel's original engine injected fuel with the assistance of compressed air, which atomized the fuel and forced it into the engine through a nozzle (a similar principle to an aerosol spray). The nozzle opening was closed by a pin valve lifted by the camshaft to initiate the fuel injection before top dead centre (<a href="/wiki/Top_Dead_Center" title="Top Dead Center" class="mw-redirect">TDC</a>). This is called an <b>air-blast injection</b>. Driving the three stage compressor used some power but the efficiency and net power output was more than any other combustion engine at that time.</p>
<p>Diesel engines in service today raise the fuel to extreme pressures by mechanical pumps and deliver it to the combustion chamber by pressure-activated injectors without compressed air. With direct injected diesels, injectors spray fuel through 4 to 12 small orifices in its nozzle. The early air injection diesels always had a superior combustion without the sharp increase in pressure during combustion. Research is now being performed and patents are being taken out to again use some form of air injection to reduce the nitrogen oxides and pollution, reverting to Diesel's original implementation with its superior combustion and possibly quieter operation. In all major aspects, the modern diesel engine holds true to Rudolf Diesel's original design, that of igniting fuel by compression at an extremely high pressure within the cylinder. With much higher pressures and high technology injectors, present-day diesel engines use the so-called solid injection system applied by <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Akroyd_Stuart" title="Herbert Akroyd Stuart">Herbert Akroyd Stuart</a> for his <a href="/wiki/Hot_bulb_engine" title="Hot bulb engine">hot bulb engine</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Indirect_injection" title="Indirect injection">indirect injection</a> engine could be considered the latest development of these low speed <i>hot bulb</i> ignition engines.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel_delivery">Fuel delivery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Fuel delivery">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Fuel delivery" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A vital component of all diesel engines is a mechanical or electronic <a href="/wiki/Governor_(device)" title="Governor (device)">governor</a> which regulates the idling speed and maximum speed of the engine by controlling the rate of fuel delivery. Unlike Otto-cycle engines, incoming air is not throttled and a diesel engine without a governor cannot have a stable idling speed and can easily overspeed, resulting in its destruction. Mechanically governed fuel injection systems are driven by the engine's <a href="/wiki/Gear_train" title="Gear train">gear train</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-buckman_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buckman-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> These systems use a combination of springs and weights to control fuel delivery relative to both load and speed.<sup id="cite_ref-buckman_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buckman-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> Modern electronically controlled diesel engines control fuel delivery by use of an electronic control module (ECM) or electronic control unit (<a href="/wiki/Electronic_control_unit" title="Electronic control unit">ECU</a>). The ECM/ECU receives an engine speed signal, as well as other operating parameters such as intake manifold pressure and fuel temperature, from a sensor and controls the amount of fuel and start of injection timing through <a href="/wiki/Actuator" title="Actuator">actuators</a> to maximise power and efficiency and minimise emissions. Controlling the timing of the start of injection of fuel into the cylinder is a key to minimizing emissions, and maximizing <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_automobiles" title="Fuel economy in automobiles">fuel economy</a> (efficiency), of the engine. The timing is measured in degrees of crank angle of the <a href="/wiki/Piston" title="Piston">piston</a> before top dead centre. For example, if the ECM/ECU initiates fuel injection when the piston is 10° before <a href="/w/index.php?title=Top_Dead_Centre&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Top Dead Centre (page does not exist)">TDC</a>, the start of injection, or timing, is said to be 10° <a href="/wiki/BTDC" title="BTDC" class="mw-redirect">BTDC</a>. Optimal timing will depend on the engine design as well as its speed and load.</p>
<p>Advancing the start of injection (injecting before the piston reaches to its SOI-TDC) results in higher in-cylinder pressure and temperature, and higher efficiency, but also results in elevated engine noise and increased <a href="/wiki/Oxides_of_nitrogen" title="Oxides of nitrogen" class="mw-redirect">oxides of nitrogen</a> (NO<sub>x</sub>) emissions due to higher combustion temperatures. Delaying start of injection causes incomplete combustion, reduced fuel efficiency and an increase in exhaust smoke, containing a considerable amount of particulate matter and unburned <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_advantages">Major advantages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Major advantages">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Major advantages" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Diesel engines have several advantages over other internal combustion engines:</p>
<ul>
<li>They burn less fuel than a petrol engine performing the same work, due to the engine's higher temperature of combustion and greater expansion ratio.<sup id="cite_ref-bmmWW_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bmmWW-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> Gasoline engines are typically 30% efficient while diesel engines can convert over 45% of the fuel energy into mechanical energy<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> (see <a href="/wiki/Carnot_cycle" title="Carnot cycle">Carnot cycle</a> for further explanation).</li>
<li>They have no high voltage electrical ignition system, resulting in high reliability and easy adaptation to damp environments. The absence of coils, spark plug wires, etc., also eliminates a source of radio frequency emissions which can interfere with navigation and communication equipment, which is especially important in marine and aircraft applications.</li>
<li>The life of a diesel engine is generally about twice as long as that of a petrol engine<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (June 2013)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> due to the increased strength of parts used. Diesel fuel has better lubrication properties than petrol as well.</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Soybeanbus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Soybeanbus.jpg/220px-Soybeanbus.jpg" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Soybeanbus.jpg/330px-Soybeanbus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Soybeanbus.jpg/440px-Soybeanbus.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Soybeanbus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Bus powered by <b>biodiesel</b></div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">Diesel fuel</a> is distilled directly from petroleum. Distillation yields some gasoline, but the yield would be inadequate without <a href="/wiki/Catalytic_reforming" title="Catalytic reforming">catalytic reforming</a>, which is a more costly process.</li>
<li>Diesel fuel is considered safer than petrol in many applications. Although diesel fuel will burn in open air using a <a href="/wiki/Candle_wick" title="Candle wick">wick</a>, it will not explode and does not release a large amount of flammable vapor. The low vapor pressure of diesel is especially advantageous in marine applications, where the accumulation of explosive fuel-air mixtures is a particular hazard. For the same reason, diesel engines are immune to <a href="/wiki/Vapor_lock" title="Vapor lock">vapor lock</a>.</li>
<li>For any given partial load the fuel efficiency (mass burned per energy produced) of a diesel engine remains nearly constant, as opposed to petrol and turbine engines which use proportionally more fuel with partial power outputs.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>They generate less waste heat in cooling and exhaust.<sup id="cite_ref-bmmWW_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bmmWW-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Diesel engines can accept super- or turbo-charging pressure without any natural limit, constrained only by the strength of engine components. This is unlike petrol engines, which inevitably suffer detonation at higher pressure.</li>
<li>The carbon monoxide content of the exhaust is minimal, therefore diesel engines are used in underground mines.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">Biodiesel</a> is an easily synthesized, non-petroleum-based fuel (through <a href="/wiki/Transesterification" title="Transesterification">transesterification</a>) which can run directly in many diesel engines, while gasoline engines either need adaptation to run <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_fuel" title="Synthetic fuel">synthetic fuels</a> or else use them as an additive to gasoline (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> added to <a href="/wiki/Gasohol" title="Gasohol" class="mw-redirect">gasohol</a>).</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanical_and_electronic_injection">Mechanical and electronic injection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Mechanical and electronic injection">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Mechanical and electronic injection" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many configurations of fuel injection have been used over the course of the 20th century.</p>
<p>Most present-day diesel engines use a mechanical single plunger high-pressure <a href="/wiki/Fuel_pump" title="Fuel pump">fuel pump</a> driven by the engine crankshaft. For each engine cylinder, the corresponding plunger in the fuel pump measures out the correct amount of fuel and determines the timing of each injection. These engines use <a href="/wiki/Injectors" title="Injectors" class="mw-redirect">injectors</a> that are very precise spring-loaded valves that open and close at a specific fuel pressure. Separate high-pressure fuel lines connect the fuel pump with each cylinder. Fuel volume for each single combustion is controlled by a slanted <a href="/wiki/Groove_(machining)" title="Groove (machining)" class="mw-redirect">groove</a> in the plunger which rotates only a few degrees releasing the pressure and is controlled by a mechanical governor, consisting of weights rotating at engine speed constrained by springs and a lever. The injectors are held open by the fuel pressure. On high-speed engines the plunger pumps are together in one unit.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> The length of fuel lines from the pump to each injector is normally the same for each cylinder in order to obtain the same pressure delay.</p>
<p>A cheaper configuration on high-speed engines with fewer than six cylinders is to use an axial-piston distributor pump, consisting of one rotating pump plunger delivering fuel to a valve and line for each cylinder (functionally analogous to points and distributor cap on an <a href="/wiki/Otto_engine" title="Otto engine">Otto engine</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-buckman_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buckman-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Many modern systems have a single fuel pump which supplies fuel constantly at high pressure with a <a href="/wiki/Common_rail" title="Common rail">common rail</a> (single fuel line common) to each injector. Each injector has a <a href="/wiki/Solenoid" title="Solenoid">solenoid</a> operated by an electronic control unit, resulting in more accurate control of injector opening times that depend on other control conditions, such as engine speed and loading, and providing better engine performance and fuel economy.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Both mechanical and electronic injection systems can be used in either <a href="/wiki/Fuel_injection#Direct_injection" title="Fuel injection">direct</a> or <a href="/wiki/Indirect_injection" title="Indirect injection">indirect injection</a> configurations.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Two-stroke_engine" title="Two-stroke engine">Two-stroke</a> diesel engines with mechanical injection pumps can be inadvertently run in reverse, albeit in a very inefficient manner, possibly damaging the engine.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Large ship <a href="/wiki/Two-stroke_engine" title="Two-stroke engine">two-stroke</a> diesels are designed to run in either direction, obviating the need for a gearbox.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indirect_injection">Indirect injection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Indirect injection">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Indirect injection" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Diesel_Indirect_Injection_Toyota_motor.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Diesel_Indirect_Injection_Toyota_motor.jpg/170px-Diesel_Indirect_Injection_Toyota_motor.jpg" width="170" height="264" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Diesel_Indirect_Injection_Toyota_motor.jpg/255px-Diesel_Indirect_Injection_Toyota_motor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Diesel_Indirect_Injection_Toyota_motor.jpg/340px-Diesel_Indirect_Injection_Toyota_motor.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Diesel_Indirect_Injection_Toyota_motor.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Arrow indicates opening from pre-chamber</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Indirect_injection" title="Indirect injection">Indirect injection</a></div>
<p>An indirect injection diesel engine delivers fuel into a chamber off the <a href="/wiki/Combustion_chamber" title="Combustion chamber">combustion chamber</a>, called a pre-chamber or ante-chamber, where combustion begins and then spreads into the main combustion chamber, assisted by <a href="/wiki/Turbulence" title="Turbulence">turbulence</a> created in the chamber. This system allows for a smoother, quieter running engine, and because combustion is assisted by turbulence, <a href="/wiki/Injector" title="Injector">injector</a> pressures can be lower, about 100&#160;bar (10&#160;MPa; 1,500&#160;psi), using a single orifice tapered jet injector. Mechanical injection systems allowed high-speed running suitable for road vehicles (typically up to speeds of around <span style="white-space:nowrap">4,000&#160;rpm</span>. The pre-chamber had the disadvantage of increasing heat loss to the engine's cooling system, and restricting the combustion burn, which reduced the efficiency by 5–10%.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> Indirect injection engines are cheaper to build and it is easier to produce smooth, quiet-running vehicles with a simple mechanical system. In road-going vehicles most prefer the greater efficiency and better controlled emission levels of direct injection. Indirect injection diesels can still be found in the many ATV diesel applications.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Direct_injection">Direct injection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Direct injection">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Direct injection" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Fuel_injection#Direct_injection" title="Fuel injection">Direct injection</a> diesel engines have injectors mounted at the top of the combustion chamber. The injectors are activated using one of two methods - hydraulic pressure from the fuel pump, or an electronic signal from an engine controller.</p>
<p>Hydraulic pressure activated injectors can produce harsh engine noise. Fuel consumption is about 15–20% lower than indirect injection diesels. The extra noise is generally not a problem for industrial uses of the engine, but for automotive usage, buyers have to decide whether or not the increased fuel efficiency would compensate for the extra noise.</p>
<p>Electronic control of the fuel injection transformed the direct injection engine by allowing much greater control over the combustion.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unit_direct_injection">Unit direct injection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Unit direct injection">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Unit direct injection" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Unit_Injector" title="Unit Injector">Unit Injector</a></div>
<p>Unit direct injection also injects fuel directly into the cylinder of the engine. In this system the injector and the pump are combined into one unit positioned over each cylinder controlled by the camshaft. Each cylinder has its own unit eliminating the high-pressure fuel lines, achieving a more consistent injection. This type of injection system, also developed by Bosch, is used by <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen" title="Volkswagen">Volkswagen</a> AG in cars (where it is called a <i><a href="/wiki/Pumpe-D%C3%BCse" title="Pumpe-Düse" class="mw-redirect">Pumpe-Düse</a>-System</i>—literally <i>pump-nozzle system</i>) and by Mercedes Benz ("PLD") and most major diesel engine manufacturers in large commercial engines (<a href="/wiki/Caterpillar_Inc." title="Caterpillar Inc.">CAT</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cummins" title="Cummins">Cummins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Diesel" title="Detroit Diesel">Detroit Diesel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electro-Motive_Diesel" title="Electro-Motive Diesel">Electro-Motive Diesel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Volvo" title="Volvo">Volvo</a>). With recent advancements, the pump pressure has been raised to 2,400 bars (240&#160;MPa; 35,000&#160;psi),<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> allowing injection parameters similar to common rail systems.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Common_rail_direct_injection">Common rail direct injection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Common rail direct injection">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Common rail direct injection" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Common_rail" title="Common rail">Common rail</a></div>
<p>In common rail systems, the separate pulsing high-pressure fuel line to each cylinder's injector is also eliminated. Instead, a high-pressure pump pressurizes fuel at up to 2,500&#160;bar (250&#160;MPa; 36,000&#160;psi),<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> in a "common rail". The common rail is a tube that supplies each computer-controlled injector containing a precision-machined nozzle and a plunger driven by a <a href="/wiki/Solenoid" title="Solenoid">solenoid</a> or <a href="/wiki/Piezoelectricity" title="Piezoelectricity">piezoelectric</a> actuator.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cold_weather">Cold weather</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Cold weather">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Cold weather" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Starting">Starting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Starting">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Starting" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In cold weather, high speed diesel engines can be difficult to start because the mass of the cylinder block and cylinder head absorb the heat of compression, preventing ignition due to the higher surface-to-volume ratio. Pre-chambered engines make use of small electric heaters inside the pre-chambers called <a href="/wiki/Glowplug" title="Glowplug">glowplugs</a>, while the direct-injected engines have these <a href="/wiki/Glowplug" title="Glowplug">glowplugs</a> in the combustion chamber.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Many engines use resistive heaters in the <a href="/wiki/Intake_manifold" title="Intake manifold" class="mw-redirect">intake manifold</a> to warm the inlet air for starting, or until the engine reaches <a href="/wiki/Operating_temperature" title="Operating temperature">operating temperature</a>. <a href="/wiki/Engine_block_heater" title="Engine block heater" class="mw-redirect">Engine block heaters</a> (electric resistive heaters in the engine block) connected to the utility grid are used in cold climates when an engine is turned off for extended periods (more than an hour), to reduce startup time and engine wear. Block heaters are also used for emergency power <a href="/wiki/Standby_generator" title="Standby generator">standby Diesel-powered generators</a> which must rapidly pick up load on a power failure. In the past, a wider variety of cold-start methods were used. Some engines, such as <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Diesel" title="Detroit Diesel">Detroit Diesel</a><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> engines used<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (September 2010)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> a system to introduce small amounts of <a href="/wiki/Ether" title="Ether">ether</a> into the inlet manifold to start combustion. Others used a mixed system, with a resistive heater burning <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>. An impromptu method, particularly on out-of-tune engines, is to manually spray an aerosol can of ether-based engine starter fluid into the intake air stream (usually through the intake air filter assembly).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gelling">Gelling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Gelling">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Gelling" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Diesel fuel is also prone to <i>waxing</i> or <i>gelling</i> in cold weather; both are terms for the solidification of diesel oil into a partially crystalline state. The crystals build up in the fuel line (especially in fuel filters), eventually starving the engine of fuel and causing it to stop running. Low-output electric heaters in <a href="/wiki/Fuel_tank" title="Fuel tank">fuel tanks</a> and around fuel lines are used to solve this problem. Also, most engines have a <i>spill return</i> system, by which any excess fuel from the injector pump and injectors is returned to the fuel tank. Once the engine has warmed, returning warm fuel prevents waxing in the tank.</p>
<p>Due to improvements in fuel technology with additives, waxing rarely occurs in all but the coldest weather when a mix of diesel and <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a> may be used to run a vehicle. Gas stations in regions with a cold climate are required to offer <a href="/wiki/Winter_diesel_fuel" title="Winter diesel fuel">winterized diesel</a> in the cold seasons that allow operation below a specific <a href="/wiki/Cold_filter_plugging_point" title="Cold filter plugging point">Cold Filter Plugging Point</a>. In Europe these diesel characteristics are described in the <a href="/wiki/EN_590" title="EN 590">EN 590</a> standard.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Types">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Types" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size_Groups">Size Groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Size Groups">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Size Groups" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Diesel_engine_(PSF).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Diesel_engine_%28PSF%29.png/230px-Diesel_engine_%28PSF%29.png" width="230" height="302" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Diesel_engine_%28PSF%29.png/345px-Diesel_engine_%28PSF%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Diesel_engine_%28PSF%29.png/460px-Diesel_engine_%28PSF%29.png 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Diesel_engine_(PSF).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Two Cycle Diesel engine with <a href="/wiki/Roots_blower" title="Roots blower" class="mw-redirect">Roots blower</a>, typical of <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Diesel" title="Detroit Diesel">Detroit Diesel</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Electro-Motive_Diesel" title="Electro-Motive Diesel">Electro-Motive Diesel</a> Engines</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are three size groups of Diesel engines<sup id="cite_ref-Brit2and4_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brit2and4-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Small - Under 188&#160;kW (252&#160;hp) output</li>
<li>Medium</li>
<li>Large</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Basic_Types_of_Diesel_Engines">Basic Types of Diesel Engines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Basic Types of Diesel Engines">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Basic Types of Diesel Engines" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are two basic types of Diesel Engines<sup id="cite_ref-Brit2and4_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brit2and4-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Four Cycle</li>
<li>Two Cycle</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early">Early</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Early">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Early" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Rudolf Diesel based his engine on the design of the Gas engine created by Nikolaus Otto in 1876 with the goal of improving its efficiency. He patented his Diesel engine concepts in patents that were set forth in 1892 and 1893.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> As such, diesel engines in the late 19th and early 20th centuries used the same basic layout and form as industrial steam engines, with long-bore cylinders, external valve gear, cross-head bearings and an open crankshaft connected to a large <a href="/wiki/Flywheel" title="Flywheel">flywheel</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Disputed statement" class="mw-redirect"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (May 2011)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Diesel_engine#Dubious" title="Talk:Diesel engine">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> Smaller engines would be built with vertical cylinders, while most medium- and large-sized industrial engines were built with horizontal cylinders, just as steam engines had been. Engines could be built with more than one cylinder in both cases. The largest early diesels resembled the triple-expansion steam <a href="/wiki/Reciprocating_engine" title="Reciprocating engine">reciprocating engine</a>, being tens of feet high with vertical cylinders arranged in-line. These early engines ran at very slow speeds—partly due to the limitations of their air-blast injector equipment and partly so they would be compatible with the majority of industrial equipment designed for steam engines; maximum speeds of 100–300&#160;<a href="/wiki/Revolutions_per_minute" title="Revolutions per minute">rpm</a> were common. Engines were usually started by allowing compressed air into the cylinders to turn the engine, although smaller engines could be started by hand.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1897 when the first Diesel engine was completed Adolphus Busch traveled to Cologne and negotiated exclusive right to produce the Diesel engine in the USA and Canada. In his examination of the engine it was noted that the Diesel at that time operated at efficiencies of 32–35% thermodynamic efficiency when a typical triple expansion steam engine would operate at about 18%.<sup id="cite_ref-Busch_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Busch-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the early decades of the 20th century, when large diesel engines were first being used, the engines took a form similar to the compound steam engines common at the time, with the piston being connected to the connecting rod by a <a href="/wiki/Crosshead_bearing" title="Crosshead bearing" class="mw-redirect">crosshead bearing</a>. Following steam engine practice some manufacturers made double-acting two-stroke and four-stroke diesel engines to increase power output, with combustion taking place on both sides of the piston, with two sets of valve gear and fuel injection. While it produced large amounts of power and was very efficient, the double-acting diesel engine's main problem was producing a good seal where the piston rod passed through the bottom of the lower combustion chamber to the crosshead bearing, and no more were built. By the 1930s turbochargers were fitted to some engines. Crosshead bearings are still used to reduce the wear on the cylinders in large long-stroke main marine engines.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_High-_and_Medium-speed_Engines">Modern High- and Medium-speed Engines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Modern High- and Medium-speed Engines">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Modern High- and Medium-speed Engines" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Yanmar_2GM20.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Yanmar_2GM20.JPG/220px-Yanmar_2GM20.JPG" width="220" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Yanmar_2GM20.JPG/330px-Yanmar_2GM20.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Yanmar_2GM20.JPG/440px-Yanmar_2GM20.JPG 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Yanmar_2GM20.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Yanmar_2GM20" title="Yanmar 2GM20">Yanmar 2GM20</a> marine diesel engine, installed in a <a href="/wiki/Sailboat" title="Sailboat">sailboat</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As with petrol engines, there are two classes of diesel engines in current use: two-stroke and four-stroke. The four-stroke type is the "classic" version, tracing its lineage back to Rudolf Diesel's <a href="/wiki/Prototype" title="Prototype">prototype</a>. It is also the most commonly used form, being the preferred power source for many motor vehicles, especially buses and trucks. Much larger engines, such as used for <a href="/wiki/Diesel_locomotive" title="Diesel locomotive">railroad locomotion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marine_propulsion" title="Marine propulsion">marine propulsion</a>, are often two-stroke units, offering a more favourable <a href="/wiki/Power-to-weight_ratio" title="Power-to-weight ratio">power-to-weight ratio</a>, as well as better fuel economy. The most powerful engines in the world are two-stroke diesels of mammoth dimensions.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
