<p><a href="/wiki/Two-stroke_diesel_engine" title="Two-stroke diesel engine">Two-stroke diesel engine</a> operation is similar to that of petrol counterparts, except that fuel is not mixed with air before induction, and the crankcase does not take an active role in the cycle. The traditional two-stroke design relies upon a mechanically driven <a href="/wiki/Roots_blower" title="Roots blower" class="mw-redirect">positive displacement blower</a> to charge the cylinders with air before compression and ignition. The charging process also assists in expelling (scavenging) <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a> gases remaining from the previous power stroke.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Archetype" title="Archetype">archetype</a> of the modern form of the two-stroke diesel is the (high-speed) <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Diesel_Series_71" title="Detroit Diesel Series 71">Detroit Diesel Series 71</a> engine, designed by <a href="/wiki/Charles_F._Kettering" title="Charles F. Kettering">Charles F. "Boss" Kettering</a> and his colleagues at <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors Corporation</a> in 1938, in which the blower pressurizes a chamber in the engine block that is often referred to as the "air box". The (very much larger medium-speed) <a href="/wiki/Electro-Motive_Diesel" title="Electro-Motive Diesel">Electro-Motive Diesel</a> engine<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> is used as the <a href="/wiki/Prime_mover_(locomotive)" title="Prime mover (locomotive)">prime mover</a> in EMD <a href="/wiki/Diesel-electric_locomotive" title="Diesel-electric locomotive" class="mw-redirect">diesel-electric locomotive</a>, marine and stationary applications, and was designed by the same team, and is built to the same principle. However, a significant improvement built into most later EMD engines is the mechanically-assisted turbo-compressor, which provides charge air using mechanical assistance during starting (thereby obviating the necessity for <a href="/wiki/Roots_supercharger" title="Roots supercharger" class="mw-redirect">Roots-blown</a> scavenging), and provides charge air using an exhaust gas-driven turbine during normal operations—thereby providing true turbocharging and additionally increasing the engine's power output by at least fifty percent.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>a<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In a two-stroke diesel engine, as the cylinder's <a href="/wiki/Piston" title="Piston">piston</a> approaches the bottom dead centre exhaust ports or valves are opened relieving most of the excess pressure after which a passage between the air box and the cylinder is opened, permitting air flow into the cylinder.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> The air flow blows the remaining combustion gases from the cylinder—this is the scavenging process. As the piston passes through bottom centre and starts upward, the passage is closed and compression commences,<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> culminating in fuel injection and ignition.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> Refer to <a href="/wiki/Two-stroke#Two-stroke_diesel_engines" title="Two-stroke" class="mw-redirect">two-stroke diesel engines</a> for more detailed coverage of aspiration types and supercharging of two-stroke diesel engines.</p>
<p>Normally, the number of cylinders are used in multiples of two, although any number of cylinders can be used as long as the load on the crankshaft is counterbalanced to prevent excessive <a href="/wiki/Oscillation" title="Oscillation">vibration</a>. The inline-six-cylinder design is the most prolific in light- to medium-duty engines, though small V8 and larger inline-four displacement engines are also common. Small-capacity engines (generally considered to be those below five litres in capacity) are generally four- or six-cylinder types, with the four-cylinder being the most common type found in automotive uses. Five-cylinder diesel engines have also been produced, being a compromise between the smooth running of the six-cylinder and the space-efficient dimensions of the four-cylinder. Diesel engines for smaller plant machinery, boats, tractors, generators and pumps may be four-, three- or two-cylinder types, with the single-cylinder diesel engine remaining for light stationary work. Direct reversible two-stroke marine diesels need at least three cylinders for reliable restarting forwards and reverse, while four-stroke diesels need at least six cylinders.</p>
<p>The desire to improve the diesel engine's <a href="/wiki/Power-to-weight" title="Power-to-weight" class="mw-redirect">power-to-weight</a> ratio produced several novel cylinder arrangements to extract more power from a given capacity. The uniflow <a href="/wiki/Opposed-piston_engine" title="Opposed-piston engine">opposed-piston engine</a> uses two pistons in one cylinder with the combustion cavity in the middle and gas in- and outlets at the ends. This makes a comparatively light, powerful, swiftly running and economic engine suitable for use in aviation. An example is the <a href="/wiki/Junkers_Jumo_204" title="Junkers Jumo 204">Junkers Jumo 204/205</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Napier_Deltic" title="Napier Deltic">Napier Deltic</a> engine, with three cylinders arranged in a triangular formation, each containing two opposed pistons, the whole engine having three crankshafts, is one of the better known.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_Low-speed_Engines">Modern Low-speed Engines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Modern Low-speed Engines">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Modern Low-speed Engines" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Low-speed diesel engines (as used in ships and other applications where overall engine weight is relatively unimportant) often have a thermal efficiency which exceeds 50%.<sup id="cite_ref-bmmWW_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bmmWW-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mhi_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mhi-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gas_generator">Gas generator</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Gas generator">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Gas generator" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Free-piston_engine" title="Free-piston engine">Free-piston engine</a></div>
<p>Before 1950, <a href="/wiki/Sulzer_(manufacturer)" title="Sulzer (manufacturer)">Sulzer</a> started experimenting with two-stroke engines with boost pressures as high as 6 <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_(unit)" title="Atmosphere (unit)">atmospheres</a>, in which all the output power was taken from an exhaust <a href="/wiki/Gas_turbine" title="Gas turbine">gas turbine</a>. The two-stroke pistons directly drove air compressor pistons to make a positive displacement gas generator. Opposed pistons were connected by linkages instead of crankshafts. Several of these units could be connected to provide power gas to one large output turbine. The overall thermal efficiency was roughly twice that of a simple gas turbine.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> This system was derived from <a href="/wiki/Ra%C3%BAl_Pateras_Pescara" title="Raúl Pateras Pescara">Raúl Pateras Pescara</a>'s work on free-piston engines in the 1930s.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Advantages_and_disadvantages_versus_spark-ignition_engines">Advantages and disadvantages versus spark-ignition engines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Advantages and disadvantages versus spark-ignition engines">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Advantages and disadvantages versus spark-ignition engines" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" style="" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width: 52px;"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" /></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text" style=""><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for verification</b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>.
