<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_and_fuel_economy">Power and fuel economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Power and fuel economy">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Power and fuel economy" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/MAN_B%26W_Diesel" title="MAN B&amp;W Diesel" class="mw-redirect">MAN</a> S80ME-C7 low speed diesel engines use 155 grams (5.5&#160;oz) of fuel per <a href="/wiki/KWh" title="KWh" class="mw-redirect">kWh</a> for an overall energy conversion efficiency of 54.4%, which is the highest conversion of fuel into power by any single-cycle <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion" title="Internal combustion" class="mw-redirect">internal</a> or <a href="/wiki/External_combustion" title="External combustion" class="mw-redirect">external combustion</a> engine<sup id="cite_ref-bmmWW_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bmmWW-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> (The efficiency of a combined cycle <a href="/wiki/Gas_turbine" title="Gas turbine">gas turbine</a> system can exceed 60%.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup>) Diesel engines are more efficient than gasoline (petrol) engines of the same power rating, resulting in lower fuel consumption. A common margin is 40% more <a href="/wiki/Miles_per_gallon" title="Miles per gallon" class="mw-redirect">miles per gallon</a> for an efficient <a href="/wiki/Turbodiesel" title="Turbodiesel" class="mw-redirect">turbodiesel</a>. For example, the current model <a href="/wiki/%C5%A0koda_Octavia" title="Škoda Octavia">Škoda Octavia</a>, using <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen_Group" title="Volkswagen Group">Volkswagen Group</a> engines, has a combined Euro rating of 6.2&#160;L/100&#160;km (46&#160;mpg<sub><small>-imp</small></sub>; 38&#160;mpg<sub><small>-US</small></sub>) for the 102&#160;<a href="/wiki/Horsepower#Brake_horsepower" title="Horsepower">bhp</a> (76&#160;kW) petrol engine and 4.4&#160;L/100&#160;km (64&#160;mpg<sub><small>-imp</small></sub>; 53&#160;mpg<sub><small>-US</small></sub>) for the 105&#160;bhp (78&#160;kW) diesel engine.</p>
<p>However, such a comparison does not take into account that diesel fuel is denser and contains about 15% more energy by volume. Although the <a href="/wiki/Heat_of_combustion" title="Heat of combustion">calorific value</a> of the fuel is slightly lower at 45.3&#160;MJ/kg (<a href="/wiki/Joules" title="Joules" class="mw-redirect">megajoules</a> per kilogram) than petrol at 45.8&#160;MJ/kg, liquid diesel fuel is significantly denser than liquid petrol. This is significant because volume of fuel, in addition to mass, is an important consideration in mobile applications. No vehicle has an unlimited volume available for fuel storage.</p>
<p>Adjusting the numbers to account for the energy density of diesel fuel, the overall energy efficiency is still about 20% greater for the diesel version.</p>
<p>While a higher compression ratio is helpful in raising efficiency, diesel engines are much more efficient than gasoline (petrol) engines when at low power and at engine idle. Unlike the petrol engine, diesels lack a butterfly valve (throttle) in the inlet system, which closes at idle. This creates parasitic loss and destruction of availability of the incoming air, reducing the efficiency of petrol engines at idle. In many applications, such as marine, agriculture, and railways, diesels are left idling and unattended for many hours, sometimes even days. These advantages are especially attractive in locomotives (see <a href="/wiki/Dieselisation" title="Dieselisation">dieselisation</a>).</p>
<p>The average diesel engine has a poorer power-to-weight ratio than the <a href="/wiki/Petrol_engine" title="Petrol engine">petrol engine</a>. This is because the diesel must operate at lower engine speeds<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> and because it needs heavier, stronger parts to resist the operating pressure caused by the high compression ratio of the engine and the large amounts of torque generated to the crankshaft. In addition, diesels are often built with stronger parts to give them longer lives and better reliability, important considerations in industrial applications.</p>
<p>For most industrial or nautical applications, reliability is considered more important than light weight and high power. Diesel fuel is injected just before the power stroke. As a result, the fuel cannot burn completely unless it has a sufficient amount of oxygen. This can result in incomplete combustion and black smoke in the exhaust if more fuel is injected than there is air available for the combustion process. Modern engines with electronic fuel delivery can adjust the timing and amount of fuel delivery (by changing the duration of the injection pulse), and so operate with less waste of fuel. In a mechanical system, the injection timing and duration must be set to be efficient at the anticipated operating rpm and load, and so the settings are less than ideal when the engine is running at any other RPM than what it is timed for. The electronic injection can "sense" engine revs, load, even boost and temperature, and continuously alter the timing to match the given situation. In the petrol engine, air and fuel are mixed for the entire compression stroke, ensuring complete mixing even at higher engine speeds.</p>
<p>Diesel engines usually have longer stroke lengths chiefly to facilitate achieving the necessary compression ratios, but also to reduce the optimal operating speed (rpm). As a result piston and connecting rods are heavier and more force must be transmitted through the connecting rods and crankshaft to change the momentum of the piston. This is another reason that a diesel engine must be stronger for the same power output as a petrol engine.</p>
<p>Yet it is this characteristic that has allowed some enthusiasts to acquire significant power increases with <a href="/wiki/Turbocharger" title="Turbocharger">turbocharged</a> engines by making fairly simple and inexpensive modifications. A petrol engine of similar size cannot put out a comparable power increase without extensive alterations because the stock components cannot withstand the higher stresses placed upon them. Since a diesel engine is already built to withstand higher levels of stress, it makes an ideal candidate for <a href="/wiki/Engine_tuning" title="Engine tuning">performance tuning</a> at little expense. However, it should be said that any modification that raises the amount of fuel and air put through a diesel engine will increase its operating temperature, which will reduce its life and increase service requirements. These are issues with newer, lighter, <i>high-performance</i> diesel engines which are not "overbuilt" to the degree of older engines and they are being pushed to provide greater power in smaller engines.</p>
<p>The addition of a <a href="/wiki/Turbocharger" title="Turbocharger">turbocharger</a> or <a href="/wiki/Supercharger" title="Supercharger">supercharger</a> to the engine greatly assists in increasing <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_automobiles" title="Fuel economy in automobiles">fuel economy</a> and power output, mitigating the fuel-air intake speed limit mentioned above for a given engine displacement. Boost pressures can be higher on diesels than on petrol engines, due to the latter's susceptibility to knock, and the higher <a href="/wiki/Compression_ratio" title="Compression ratio">compression ratio</a> allows a diesel engine to be more efficient than a comparable spark ignition engine. Because the burned gases are expanded further in a diesel engine cylinder, the exhaust gas is cooler, meaning turbochargers require less cooling, and can be more reliable, than with spark-ignition engines.</p>
<p>Without the risk of knocking, boost pressure in a diesel engine can be much higher; it is possible to run as much boost as the engine will physically stand before breaking apart.</p>
<p>The increased fuel economy of the diesel engine over the petrol engine means that the diesel produces less <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (CO<sub>2</sub>) per unit distance. Recent advances in production and changes in the political climate have increased the availability and awareness of <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a>, an alternative to petroleum-derived diesel fuel with a much lower net-sum emission of CO<sub>2</sub>, due to the absorption of CO<sub>2</sub> by plants used to produce the fuel. Although concerns are now being raised as to the negative effect this is having on the world food supply, as the growing of crops specifically for <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a> takes up land that could be used for food crops and uses water that could be used by both humans and animals. However, the use of waste vegetable oil, sawmill waste from managed forests in Finland, and advances in the production of vegetable oil from algae demonstrate great promise in providing feed stocks for sustainable biodiesel that are not in competition with food production.</p>
<p>A combination of improved mechanical technology (such as multi-stage injectors which fire a short "pilot charge" of fuel into the cylinder to warm the combustion chamber before delivering the main fuel charge), higher injection pressures that have improved the atomisation of fuel into smaller droplets, and electronic control (which can adjust the timing and length of the injection process to optimise it for all speeds and temperatures) have mitigated most of these problems in the latest generation of common-rail designs, while greatly improving engine efficiency. Poor power and narrow torque bands have been addressed by superchargers, turbochargers, (especially <a href="/wiki/Variable_geometry_turbocharger" title="Variable geometry turbocharger" class="mw-redirect">variable geometry turbochargers</a>), <a href="/wiki/Intercooler" title="Intercooler">intercoolers</a>, and a large efficiency increase from about 35% for IDI to 45% for the latest engines in the last 15 years.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Even though diesel engines have a theoretical fuel efficiency of 75%,<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> in practice it is lower. Engines in large diesel trucks, buses, and newer diesel cars can achieve peak efficiencies around 45%,<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> and could reach 55% efficiency in the near future.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> However, average efficiency over a driving cycle is lower than peak efficiency. For example, it might be 37% for an engine with a peak efficiency of 44%.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emissions">Emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Emissions">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Emissions" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-move" style="" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width: 52px;"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Mergefrom.svg/50px-Mergefrom.svg.png" width="50" height="20" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Mergefrom.svg/75px-Mergefrom.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Mergefrom.svg/100px-Mergefrom.svg.png 2x" /></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text" style=""><span class="mbox-text-span">It has been suggested that <i><a href="/wiki/Egr_vs_scr" title="Egr vs scr">Egr vs scr</a></i> be <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Merging" title="Wikipedia:Merging">merged</a> into this article. (<a href="/wiki/Talk:Diesel_engine#Merger_proposal:_article_Egr_vs_scr_into_section_Emissions" title="Talk:Diesel engine">Discuss</a>) <small><i>Proposed since January 2013.</i></small> </span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Diesel_exhaust" title="Diesel exhaust">Diesel exhaust</a></div>
<p>In diesel engines, conditions in the engine differ from the spark-ignition engine, since power is directly controlled by the fuel supply, rather than by controlling the air supply. Thus when the engine runs at low power, there is enough oxygen present to burn the fuel, and diesel engines only make significant amounts of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> when running under a load.</p>
<p>Diesel exhaust is well known for its characteristic smell; but this smell in recent years has become much less because the <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> is now removed from the fuel in the <a href="/wiki/Oil_refinery" title="Oil refinery">oil refinery</a>.</p>
<p>Diesel <a href="/wiki/Exhaust_gas#Diesel_engines" title="Exhaust gas">exhaust</a> has been found to contain a <a href="/wiki/Diesel_exhaust#Chemical_components" title="Diesel exhaust">long list of toxic air contaminants</a>. Among these pollutants, <a href="/wiki/Diesel_exhaust#Particulates" title="Diesel exhaust">fine particle pollution</a> is perhaps the most important as a cause of <a href="/wiki/Diesel_exhaust#Health_effects" title="Diesel exhaust">diesel's harmful health effects</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_and_torque">Power and torque</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Power and torque">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Power and torque" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For commercial uses requiring towing, load carrying and other tractive tasks, diesel engines tend to have better <a href="/wiki/Torque" title="Torque">torque</a> characteristics than petrol engines. Diesel engines tend to have their torque peak quite low in their speed range (usually between 1600 and 2000&#160;rpm for a small-capacity unit, lower for a larger engine used in a <a href="/wiki/Truck" title="Truck">truck</a>). This provides smoother control over heavy loads when starting from rest, and, crucially, allows the diesel engine to be given higher loads at lower speeds than a petrol engine, making them much more economical for these applications. This characteristic is not so desirable in private cars, so most modern diesels used in such vehicles use electronic control, <a href="/wiki/Variable_geometry_turbocharger" title="Variable geometry turbocharger" class="mw-redirect">variable geometry turbochargers</a> and shorter piston strokes to achieve a wider spread of torque over the engine's speed range, typically peaking at around 2500–3000&#160;rpm.</p>
<p>While diesel engines tend to have more <a href="/wiki/Torque" title="Torque">torque</a> at lower engine speeds than petrol engines, diesel engines tend to have a narrower <a href="/wiki/Power_band" title="Power band">power band</a> than petrol engines. Naturally aspirated diesels tend to lack power and torque at the top of their speed range. This narrow band is a reason why a vehicle such as a truck may have a <a href="/wiki/Transmission_(mechanics)" title="Transmission (mechanics)">gearbox</a> with as many as 18 or more gears, to allow the engine's power to be used effectively at all speeds. Turbochargers tend to improve power at high engine speeds; superchargers improve power at lower speeds; and variable geometry turbochargers improve the engine's performance equally by flattening the torque curve. The <a href="/wiki/Paxman_Hi-Dyne_engine" title="Paxman Hi-Dyne engine">Paxman Hi-Dyne engine</a> was a 1950s attempt to widen the power band of a diesel engine.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Noise">Noise</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Noise">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Noise" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The characteristic noise of a diesel engine is variably called diesel clatter, diesel nailing, or diesel knock.<sup id="cite_ref-Comb_in_IC_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Comb_in_IC-56"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> Diesel clatter is caused largely by the diesel combustion process; the sudden ignition of the diesel fuel when injected into the combustion chamber causes a pressure wave. Engine designers can reduce diesel clatter through: indirect injection; pilot or pre-injection; injection timing; injection rate; compression ratio; turbo boost; and exhaust gas recirculation (EGR).<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> Common rail diesel injection systems permit multiple injection events as an aid to noise reduction. Diesel fuels with a higher cetane rating modify the combustion process and reduce diesel clatter.<sup id="cite_ref-Comb_in_IC_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Comb_in_IC-56"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> CN (<a href="/wiki/Cetane_number" title="Cetane number">Cetane number</a>) can be raised by distilling higher quality crude oil, by catalyzing a higher quality product or by using a cetane improving additive.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>A combination of improved mechanical technology such as multi-stage injectors which fire a short "pilot charge" of fuel into the cylinder to initiate combustion before delivering the main fuel charge, higher injection pressures that have improved the atomisation of fuel into smaller droplets, and electronic control (which can adjust the timing and length of the injection process to optimise it for all speeds and temperatures), have partially mitigated these problems in the latest generation of common-rail designs, while improving engine efficiency.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reliability">Reliability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Reliability">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Reliability" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The lack of an electrical <a href="/wiki/Ignition_system" title="Ignition system">ignition system</a> greatly improves the reliability. The high durability of a diesel engine is also due to its overbuilt nature (see above), a benefit that is magnified by the lower rotating speeds in diesels. Diesel fuel is a better lubricant than petrol so is less harmful to the oil film on <a href="/wiki/Piston_ring" title="Piston ring">piston rings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cylinder_(engine)" title="Cylinder (engine)">cylinder</a> bores; it is routine for diesel engines to cover 400,000 km (250,000&#160;mi) or more without a rebuild.</p>
<p>Due to the greater compression ratio and the increased weight of the stronger components, starting a diesel engine is harder than starting a gasoline engine of similar design and displacement. More <a href="/wiki/Torque" title="Torque">torque</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (February 2012)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> is required to push the engine through compression.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Either an electrical <a href="/wiki/Automobile_self_starter" title="Automobile self starter" class="mw-redirect">starter</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Air-start_system" title="Air-start system">air-start system</a> is used to start the engine turning. On large engines, pre-<a href="/wiki/Lubrication" title="Lubrication">lubrication</a> and slow turning of an engine, as well as heating, are required to minimise the amount of engine damage during initial start-up and running. Some smaller military diesels can be started with an explosive cartridge, called a <a href="/wiki/Coffman_engine_starter" title="Coffman engine starter">Coffman starter</a>, which provides the extra power required to get the machine turning. In the past, <a href="/wiki/Caterpillar_Inc." title="Caterpillar Inc.">Caterpillar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deere_%26_Company" title="Deere &amp; Company" class="mw-redirect">John Deere</a> used a small petrol <i>pony</i> engine in their tractors to start the primary diesel engine. The pony engine heated the diesel to aid in ignition and used a small clutch and transmission to spin up the diesel engine. Even more unusual was an <a href="/wiki/International_Harvester" title="International Harvester">International Harvester</a> design in which the diesel engine had its own carburetor and ignition system, and started on petrol. Once warmed up, the operator moved two levers to switch the engine to diesel operation, and work could begin. These engines had very complex cylinder heads, with their own petrol combustion chambers, and were vulnerable to expensive damage if special care was not taken (especially in letting the engine cool before turning it off).<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quality_and_variety_of_fuels">Quality and variety of fuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Quality and variety of fuels">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Quality and variety of fuels" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Petrol/gasoline engines are limited in the variety and quality of the fuels they can burn. Older petrol engines fitted with a <a href="/wiki/Carburetor" title="Carburetor">carburetor</a> required a volatile fuel that would vaporise easily to create the necessary <a href="/wiki/Air-fuel_ratio" title="Air-fuel ratio" class="mw-redirect">air-fuel ratio</a> for combustion. Because both air and fuel are admitted to the cylinder, if the <a href="/wiki/Compression_ratio" title="Compression ratio">compression ratio</a> of the engine is too high or the fuel too volatile (with too low an <a href="/wiki/Octane" title="Octane">octane</a> rating), the fuel will ignite under compression, as in a diesel engine, before the piston reaches the top of its stroke. This pre-ignition causes a power loss and over time major damage to the piston and cylinder. The need for a fuel that is volatile enough to vaporise but not too volatile (to avoid pre-ignition) means that petrol engines will only run on a narrow range of fuels. There has been some success at dual-fuel engines that use petrol and <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>, petrol and <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a>, and petrol and <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>.</p>
<p>In diesel engines, a mechanical injector system vaporizes the fuel directly into the combustion chamber or a pre-combustion chamber (as opposed to a <a href="/wiki/Aspirator_(pump)" title="Aspirator (pump)">Venturi jet</a> in a carburetor, or a <a href="/wiki/Fuel_injection" title="Fuel injection">fuel injector</a> in a fuel injection system vaporising fuel into the intake manifold or intake runners as in a petrol engine). This <i>forced vaporisation</i> means that less-volatile fuels can be used. More crucially, because only air is inducted into the cylinder in a diesel engine, the compression ratio can be much higher as there is no risk of pre-ignition provided the injection process is accurately timed. This means that cylinder temperatures are much higher in a diesel engine than a petrol engine, allowing less volatile fuels to be used.</p>
<p>Diesel fuel is a form of light fuel oil, very similar to <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a> (paraffin), but diesel engines, especially older or simple designs that lack precision electronic injection systems, can run on a wide variety of other fuels. Some of the most common alternatives are Jet A-1 type <a href="/wiki/Jet_fuel" title="Jet fuel">jet fuel</a> or <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil" title="Vegetable oil">vegetable oil</a> from a very wide variety of plants. Some engines can be run on vegetable oil without modification, and most others require fairly basic alterations. <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">Biodiesel</a> is a pure diesel-like fuel refined from vegetable oil and can be used in nearly all diesel engines. Requirements for fuels to be used in diesel engines are the ability of the fuel to flow along the fuel lines, the ability of the fuel to lubricate the injector pump and injectors adequately, and its ignition qualities (ignition delay, <a href="/wiki/Cetane_number" title="Cetane number">cetane number</a>). Inline mechanical injector pumps generally tolerate poor-quality or bio-fuels better than distributor-type pumps. Also, indirect injection engines generally run more satisfactorily on bio-fuels than direct injection engines. This is partly because an indirect injection engine has a much greater 'swirl' effect, improving vaporisation and combustion of fuel, and because (in the case of vegetable oil-type fuels) <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipid</a> depositions can condense on the cylinder walls of a direct-injection engine if combustion temperatures are too low (such as starting the engine from cold).</p>
<p>It is often reported that Diesel designed his engine to run on <a href="/wiki/Peanut_oil" title="Peanut oil">peanut oil</a>, but this is false. Patent number 608845 describes his engine as being designed to run on pulverulent solid fuel (<a href="/wiki/Coal_dust" title="Coal dust">coal dust</a>). Diesel stated in his published papers, "at the Paris Exhibition in 1900 (<i><a href="/wiki/Exposition_Universelle_(1900)" title="Exposition Universelle (1900)">Exposition Universelle</a></i>) there was shown by the Otto Company a small diesel engine, which, at the request of the French Government ran on <a href="/wiki/Arachis" title="Arachis">Arachide</a> (earth-nut or peanut) oil (see <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a>), and worked so smoothly that only a few people were aware of it. The engine was constructed for using <a href="/wiki/Mineral_oil" title="Mineral oil">mineral oil</a>, and was then worked on vegetable oil without any alterations being made. The French Government at the time thought of testing the applicability to power production of the Arachide, or earth-nut, which grows in considerable quantities in their African colonies, and can easily be cultivated there." Diesel himself later conducted related tests and appeared supportive of the idea.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Most large marine diesels run on heavy <a href="/wiki/Fuel_oil" title="Fuel oil">fuel oil</a> (sometimes called "bunker oil"), which is a thick, viscous and almost flameproof fuel which is very safe to store and cheap to buy in bulk as it is a waste product from the petroleum refining industry. The fuel must be heated to thin it out (often by the exhaust header) and is often passed through multiple injection stages to vaporise it.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel_and_fluid_characteristics">Fuel and fluid characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Fuel and fluid characteristics">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Fuel and fluid characteristics" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">Diesel fuel</a></div>
<p>Diesel engines can operate on a variety of different fuels, depending on configuration, though the eponymous <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel fuel</a> derived from <a href="/wiki/Crude_oil" title="Crude oil" class="mw-redirect">crude oil</a> is most common. The engines can work with the full spectrum of crude oil distillates, from natural gas, alcohols, petrol, <a href="/wiki/Wood_gas" title="Wood gas">wood gas</a> to the <i>fuel oils</i> from diesel oil to residual fuels.<sup id="cite_ref-DFE_LNG_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DFE_LNG-59"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The type of fuel used is a combination of service requirements, and fuel costs. Good-quality diesel fuel can be synthesised from <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil" title="Vegetable oil">vegetable oil</a> and alcohol. Diesel fuel can be made from coal or other carbon base using the <a href="/wiki/Fischer-Tropsch_process" title="Fischer-Tropsch process" class="mw-redirect">Fischer-Tropsch process</a>. <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">Biodiesel</a> is growing in popularity since it can frequently be used in unmodified engines, though production remains limited. Recently, biodiesel from coconut, which can produce a very promising coco methyl ester (CME), has characteristics which enhance lubricity and combustion giving a regular diesel engine without any modification more power, less particulate matter or black smoke, and smoother engine performance. The Philippines pioneers in the research on Coconut based CME with the help of German and American scientists. Petroleum-derived diesel is often called <i>petrodiesel</i> if there is need to distinguish the source of the fuel.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil_used_as_fuel" title="Vegetable oil used as fuel" class="mw-redirect">Pure plant oils</a> are increasingly being used as a fuel for cars, trucks and remote <a href="/wiki/Combined_heat_and_power" title="Combined heat and power" class="mw-redirect">combined heat and power</a> generation especially in Germany where hundreds of decentralised small- and medium-sized oil presses cold press oilseed, mainly <a href="/wiki/Rapeseed" title="Rapeseed">rapeseed</a>, for fuel. There is a <a href="/wiki/Deutsches_Institut_f%C3%BCr_Normung" title="Deutsches Institut für Normung">Deutsches Institut für Normung</a> fuel standard for rapeseed oil fuel.</p>
<p><i>Residual fuels</i> are the "dregs" of the distillation process and are a thicker, heavier oil, or oil with higher <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a>, which are so thick that they are not readily pumpable unless heated. Residual fuel oils are cheaper than clean, refined diesel oil, although they are dirtier. Their main considerations are for use in ships and very large generation sets, due to the cost of the large volume of fuel consumed, frequently amounting to many tonnes per hour. The poorly refined <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a> <a href="/wiki/Straight_vegetable_oil" title="Straight vegetable oil" class="mw-redirect">straight vegetable oil</a> (SVO) and <a href="/wiki/Waste_vegetable_oil" title="Waste vegetable oil" class="mw-redirect">waste vegetable oil</a> (WVO) can fall into this category, but can be viable fuels on non-common rail or TDI PD diesels with the simple conversion of fuel heating to 80 to 100 degrees Celsius to reduce viscosity, and adequate filtration to OEM standards. Engines using these heavy oils have to start and shut down on standard diesel fuel, as these fuels will not flow through fuel lines at low temperatures. Moving beyond that, use of low-grade fuels can lead to serious maintenance problems because of their high sulphur and lower lubrication properties. Most diesel engines that power ships like supertankers are built so that the engine can safely use low-grade fuels due to their separate cylinder and crankcase lubrication.</p>
<p>Normal diesel fuel is more difficult to ignite and slower in developing fire than petrol because of its higher <a href="/wiki/Flash_point" title="Flash point">flash point</a>, but once burning, a diesel fire can be fierce.</p>
<p>Fuel contaminants such as dirt and water are often more problematic in diesel engines than in petrol engines. Water can cause serious damage, due to corrosion, to the injection pump and injectors; and dirt, even very fine particulate matter, can damage the injection pumps due to the close tolerances that the pumps are machined to. All diesel engines will have a fuel filter (usually much finer than a filter on a petrol engine), and a water trap. The water trap (which is sometimes part of the fuel filter) often has a float connected to a warning light, which warns when there is too much water in the trap, and must be drained before damage to the engine can result. The fuel filter must be replaced much more often on a diesel engine than on a petrol engine, changing the fuel filter every 2-4 oil changes is not uncommon for some vehicles.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Safety">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Safety" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel_flammability">Fuel flammability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Fuel flammability">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Fuel flammability" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Diesel fuel has low <a href="/wiki/Flammability" title="Flammability">flammability</a>, leading to a low risk of fire caused by fuel in a vehicle equipped with a diesel engine.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Yacht" title="Yacht">yachts</a> diesels are used because petrol engines generate combustible vapors, which can accumulate in the bottom of the vessel, sometimes causing explosions. Therefore ventilation systems on petrol powered vessels are required.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">United States Army</a> and <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> use only diesel engines and turbines because of fire hazard. Although neither gasoline nor diesel is explosive in liquid form, both can create an explosive air/vapor mix under the right conditions. However, diesel fuel is less prone due to its lower <a href="/wiki/Vapor_pressure" title="Vapor pressure">vapor pressure</a>, which is an indication of evaporation rate. The Material Safety Data Sheet<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> for ultra-low sulfur diesel fuel indicates a vapor explosion hazard for diesel indoors, outdoors, or in sewers.</p>
<p>US Army gasoline-engined tanks during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> were nicknamed <a href="/wiki/Ronson%27s_Lighters" title="Ronson's Lighters" class="mw-redirect">Ronsons</a>, because of their greater likelihood of catching fire when damaged by enemy fire. (Although tank fires were usually caused by detonation of the ammunition rather than fuel).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Maintenance_hazards">Maintenance hazards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Maintenance hazards">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Maintenance hazards" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Fuel injection introduces potential hazards in engine maintenance due to the high fuel pressures used. Residual pressure can remain in the fuel lines long after an injection-equipped engine has been shut down. This residual pressure must be relieved, and if it is done so by external bleed-off, the fuel must be safely contained. If a high-pressure diesel fuel injector is removed from its seat and operated in open air, there is a risk to the operator of injury by <a href="/wiki/Jet_injector#Accidental_jet_injection" title="Jet injector">hypodermic jet-injection</a>, even with only 100 <a href="/wiki/Pounds_per_square_inch" title="Pounds per square inch">psi</a> pressure.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> The first known such injury occurred in 1937 during a diesel engine maintenance operation.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cancer">Cancer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Cancer">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Cancer" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Diesel_exhaust" title="Diesel exhaust">Diesel exhaust</a> has been classified as an <a href="/wiki/List_of_IARC_Group_1_carcinogens" title="List of IARC Group 1 carcinogens">IARC Group 1 carcinogen</a>. It is a cause of <a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">lung cancer</a> and is associated with an increased risk for <a href="/wiki/Bladder_cancer" title="Bladder cancer">bladder cancer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-PRDEE_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PRDEE-64"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diesel_applications">Diesel applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Diesel applications">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Diesel applications" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The characteristics of diesel have different advantages for different applications.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Passenger_cars">Passenger cars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Passenger cars">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Passenger cars" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Diesel engines have long been popular in bigger cars and have been used in smaller cars such as <a href="/wiki/Supermini" title="Supermini">superminis</a> like the <a href="/wiki/Peugeot_205" title="Peugeot 205">Peugeot 205</a>, in Europe since the 1980s. Diesel engines tend to be more economical at regular driving speeds and are much better at city speeds. Their reliability and life-span tend to be better (as detailed). Some 40% or more of all cars sold in Europe are diesel-powered where they are considered a low CO<sub>2</sub> option. <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz" title="Mercedes-Benz">Mercedes-Benz</a> in conjunction with <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bosch_GmbH" title="Robert Bosch GmbH">Robert Bosch GmbH</a> produced diesel-powered passenger cars starting in 1936 and very large numbers are used all over the world (often as "Grande Taxis" in the <a href="/wiki/Third_World" title="Third World">Third World</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Railroad_rolling_stock">Railroad rolling stock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Railroad rolling stock">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Railroad rolling stock" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Diesel engines have eclipsed <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engines</a> as the prime mover on all non-electrified railroads in the industrialized world. The first <a href="/wiki/Diesel_locomotive" title="Diesel locomotive">diesel locomotives</a> appeared in the early 20th century, and <a href="/wiki/Diesel_multiple_units" title="Diesel multiple units" class="mw-redirect">diesel multiple units</a> soon after.</p>
<p>While <a href="/wiki/Electric_locomotive" title="Electric locomotive">electric locomotives</a> have now replaced the <a href="/wiki/Diesel_locomotive" title="Diesel locomotive">diesel locomotive</a> almost completely on passenger traffic in Europe and Asia, diesel is still today very popular for cargo-hauling <a href="/wiki/Freight_train" title="Freight train" class="mw-redirect">freight trains</a> and on tracks where electrification is not feasible.</p>
<p>Most modern diesel locomotives are actually <a href="/wiki/Diesel-electric_locomotive" title="Diesel-electric locomotive" class="mw-redirect">diesel-electric locomotives</a>: the diesel engine is used to power an electric generator that in turn powers electric traction motors with no mechanical connection between diesel engine and traction.</p>
<p>After 2000, environmental requirements has caused higher development cost for engines, and it has become common for passenger <a href="/wiki/Multiple_unit" title="Multiple unit">multiple units</a> to use engines and automatic mechanical gearboxes made for trucks. Up to four such combinations might be used to get enough power in a train.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_transport_uses">Other transport uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Other transport uses">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Other transport uses" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Larger transport applications (<a href="/wiki/Truck" title="Truck">trucks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bus" title="Bus">buses</a>, etc.) also benefit from the Diesel's reliability and high torque output. Diesel displaced paraffin (or <a href="/wiki/Tractor_vaporising_oil" title="Tractor vaporising oil">tractor vaporising oil</a>, TVO) in most parts of the world by the end of the 1950s with the U.S. following some 20 years later.</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aircraft_Diesel_engine" title="Aircraft Diesel engine" class="mw-redirect">Aircraft</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Marine_diesel_engine#Reciprocating_diesel_engines" title="Marine diesel engine" class="mw-redirect">Marine</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Diesel_motorcycle" title="Diesel motorcycle">Motorcycles</a></li>
</ul>
<p>In merchant ships and boats, the same advantages apply with the relative safety of Diesel fuel an additional benefit. The German <a href="/wiki/Pocket_battleship" title="Pocket battleship" class="mw-redirect">pocket battleships</a> were the largest Diesel warships, but the German torpedo-boats known as <a href="/wiki/E-boat" title="E-boat">E-boats</a> (<i>Schnellboot</i>) of the Second World War were also Diesel craft. Conventional <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarines</a> have used them since before World War I, relying on the almost total absence of carbon monoxide in the exhaust. American World War II Diesel-electric submarines operated on two-stroke cycle, as opposed to the four-stroke cycle that other navies used.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_fuel_standardisation">Military fuel standardisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Military fuel standardisation">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Military fuel standardisation" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> has a single vehicle fuel policy and has selected diesel for this purpose. The use of a single fuel simplifies wartime logistics. NATO and the United States Marine Corps have even been developing a diesel military motorcycle based on a <a href="/wiki/Kawasaki_Heavy_Industries" title="Kawasaki Heavy Industries">Kawasaki</a> off road motorcycle, with a purpose designed naturally aspirated direct injection diesel at <a href="/wiki/Cranfield_University" title="Cranfield University">Cranfield University</a> in England, to be produced in the USA, because motorcycles were the last remaining gasoline-powered vehicle in their inventory. Before this, a few civilian <a href="/wiki/Motorcycle" title="Motorcycle">motorcycles</a> had been built using adapted stationary diesel engines, but the weight and cost disadvantages generally outweighed the efficiency gains.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-transport_uses">Non-transport uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Non-transport uses">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Non-transport uses" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:V12_2300HP_Power_Plant.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/V12_2300HP_Power_Plant.jpg/170px-V12_2300HP_Power_Plant.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/V12_2300HP_Power_Plant.jpg/255px-V12_2300HP_Power_Plant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/V12_2300HP_Power_Plant.jpg/340px-V12_2300HP_Power_Plant.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:V12_2300HP_Power_Plant.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
A 1944 V12 2,300&#160;kW power plant undergoing testing &amp; restoration</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Diesel engines are also used to power <a href="/wiki/Diesel_generator" title="Diesel generator">permanent</a>, portable, and backup <a href="/wiki/Engine-generator" title="Engine-generator">generators</a>, irrigation pumps,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> corn grinders,<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> and coffee de-pulpers.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Engine_speeds">Engine speeds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Engine speeds">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Engine speeds" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Within the diesel engine industry, engines are often categorized by their rotational speeds into three unofficial groups:</p>
<ul>
<li>High-speed engines (&gt; 1,000 rpm),</li>
<li>medium-speed engines (300 - 1,000 rpm), and</li>
<li>slow-speed engines (&lt; 300 rpm).</li>
</ul>
<p>High- and medium-speed engines are predominantly four-stroke engines; except for the <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Diesel" title="Detroit Diesel">Detroit Diesel</a> two-stroke range. Medium-speed engines are physically larger than high-speed engines and can burn lower-grade (slower-burning) fuel than high-speed engines. Slow-speed engines are predominantly large two-stroke crosshead engines, hence very different from high- and medium-speed engines. Due to the lower rotational speed of slow- and medium-speed engines, there is more time for combustion during the power stroke of the cycle, allowing the use of slower-burning fuels than high-speed engines.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="High-speed_engines">High-speed engines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: High-speed engines">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: High-speed engines" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>High-speed (approximately 1,000&#160;rpm and greater) engines are used to power <a href="/wiki/Truck" title="Truck">trucks</a> (lorries), <a href="/wiki/Bus" title="Bus">buses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tractor" title="Tractor">tractors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile">cars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yacht" title="Yacht">yachts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gas_compressor" title="Gas compressor">compressors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pump" title="Pump">pumps</a> and small <a href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" title="Electrical generator" class="mw-redirect">electrical generators</a>. As of 2008, most high-speed engines have <a href="/wiki/Fuel_injection#Direct_injection" title="Fuel injection">direct injection</a>. Many modern engines, particularly in on-highway applications, have <a href="/wiki/Common_rail" title="Common rail">common rail</a> <a href="/wiki/Fuel_injection#Direct_injection" title="Fuel injection">direct injection</a>, which is cleaner burning.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medium-speed_engines">Medium-speed engines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Medium-speed engines">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Medium-speed engines" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Medium-speed engines are used in large electrical generators, ship propulsion and mechanical drive applications such as large compressors or pumps. Medium speed diesel engines operate on either diesel fuel or heavy fuel oil by direct injection in the same manner as low-speed engines.</p>
<p>Engines used in electrical generators run at approximately 300 to 1000&#160;rpm and are optimized to run at a set <a href="/wiki/Synchronous_speed" title="Synchronous speed" class="mw-redirect">synchronous speed</a> depending on the generation frequency (50 or 60&#160;<a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">hertz</a>) and provide a rapid response to load changes. Typical synchronous speeds for modern medium-speed engines are 500/514&#160;rpm (50/60&#160;Hz), 600&#160;rpm (both 50 and 60&#160;Hz), 720/750&#160;rpm, and 900/1000&#160;rpm.</p>
<p>As of 2009, the largest medium-speed engines in current production have outputs up to approximately 20&#160;MW (27,000&#160;hp). and are supplied by companies like <a href="/wiki/MAN_B%26W" title="MAN B&amp;W" class="mw-redirect">MAN B&amp;W</a>, <a href="/wiki/W%C3%A4rtsil%C3%A4" title="Wärtsilä">Wärtsilä</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_plc" title="Rolls-Royce plc" class="mw-redirect">Rolls-Royce</a> (who acquired Ulstein Bergen Diesel in 1999). Most medium-speed engines produced are four-stroke machines, however there are some two-stroke medium-speed engines such as by EMD (<a href="/wiki/Electro-Motive_Diesel" title="Electro-Motive Diesel">Electro-Motive Diesel</a>), and the Fairbanks Morse OP (<a href="/wiki/Opposed-piston_engine" title="Opposed-piston engine">Opposed-piston engine</a>) type.</p>
<p>Typical cylinder bore size for medium-speed engines ranges from 20&#160;cm to 50&#160;cm, and engine configurations typically are offered ranging from in-line 4-cylinder units to V-configuration 20-cylinder units. Most larger medium-speed engines are started with compressed air direct on pistons, using an air distributor, as opposed to a pneumatic starting motor acting on the flywheel, which tends to be used for smaller engines. There is no definitive engine size cut-off point for this.</p>
<p>It should also be noted that most major manufacturers of medium-speed engines make <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>-fueled versions of their diesel engines, which in fact operate on the <a href="/wiki/Otto_cycle" title="Otto cycle">Otto cycle</a>, and require spark ignition, typically provided with a spark plug.<sup id="cite_ref-DFE_LNG_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DFE_LNG-59"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> There are also dual (diesel/natural gas/coal gas) fuel versions of medium and low speed diesel engines using a lean fuel air mixture and a small injection of diesel fuel (so-called "pilot fuel") for ignition. In case of a gas supply failure or maximum power demand these engines will instantly switch back to full diesel fuel operation.<sup id="cite_ref-DFE_LNG_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DFE_LNG-59"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Low-speed_engines">Low-speed engines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Low-speed engines">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Low-speed engines" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:5S50MC.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/5S50MC.jpg/220px-5S50MC.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/5S50MC.jpg/330px-5S50MC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/5S50MC.jpg/440px-5S50MC.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:5S50MC.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The MAN B&amp;W 5S50MC 5-cylinder, 2-stroke, low-speed marine diesel engine. This particular engine is found aboard a 29,000 tonne chemical carrier.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Also known as <i>slow-speed</i>, or traditionally <i>oil engines</i>, the largest diesel engines are primarily used to power <a href="/wiki/Ship" title="Ship">ships</a>, although there are a few land-based power generation units as well. These extremely large two-stroke engines have power outputs up to approximately 85&#160;MW (114,000&#160;hp), operate in the range from approximately 60 to 200&#160;rpm and are up to 15&#160;m (50&#160;ft) tall, and can weigh over 2,000 short tons (1,800&#160;t). They typically use direct injection running on cheap low-grade heavy fuel, also known as <i>Bunker C</i> fuel, which requires heating in the ship for tanking and before injection due to the fuel's high <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a>. Often, the waste heat recovery steam boilers attached to the engine exhaust ducting generate the heat required for fuel heating. Provided the heavy fuel system is kept warm and circulating, engines can be started and stopped on heavy fuel.</p>
<p>Large and medium marine engines are started with compressed air directly applied to the pistons. Air is applied to cylinders to start the engine forwards or backwards because they are normally directly connected to the <a href="/wiki/Propeller" title="Propeller" class="mw-redirect">propeller</a> without clutch or gearbox, and to provide reverse propulsion either the engine must be run backwards or the ship will utilise an adjustable propeller. At least three cylinders are required with <a href="/wiki/Two-stroke" title="Two-stroke" class="mw-redirect">two-stroke</a> engines and at least six cylinders with <a href="/wiki/Four-stroke" title="Four-stroke" class="mw-redirect">four-stroke</a> engines to provide <a href="/wiki/Torque" title="Torque">torque</a> every 120 degrees.</p>
<p>Companies such as <a href="/wiki/MAN_B%26W_Diesel" title="MAN B&amp;W Diesel" class="mw-redirect">MAN B&amp;W Diesel</a>, (formerly <a href="/wiki/Burmeister_%26_Wain" title="Burmeister &amp; Wain">Burmeister &amp; Wain</a>) and <a href="/wiki/W%C3%A4rtsil%C3%A4" title="Wärtsilä">Wärtsilä</a> (which acquired <a href="/wiki/Sulzer_(manufacturer)" title="Sulzer (manufacturer)">Sulzer</a> Diesel) design such large low-speed engines. They are unusually narrow and tall due to the addition of a <a href="/wiki/Crosshead_bearing" title="Crosshead bearing" class="mw-redirect">crosshead bearing</a>. As of 2007, the <a href="/wiki/Straight-14" title="Straight-14" class="mw-redirect">14-cylinder</a> <a href="/wiki/W%C3%A4rtsil%C3%A4-Sulzer_14RTFLEX96-C" title="Wärtsilä-Sulzer 14RTFLEX96-C" class="mw-redirect">Wärtsilä-Sulzer 14RTFLEX96-C</a> turbocharged two-stroke diesel engine built by <a href="/wiki/W%C3%A4rtsil%C3%A4" title="Wärtsilä">Wärtsilä</a> licensee <a href="/wiki/Doosan_Group" title="Doosan Group">Doosan</a> in Korea is the most powerful diesel engine put into service, with a cylinder bore of 960&#160;mm (37.8&#160;in) delivering 114,800 hp (85.6&#160;MW). It was put into service in September 2006, aboard the world's largest container ship <i><a href="/wiki/Emma_Maersk" title="Emma Maersk" class="mw-redirect">Emma Maersk</a></i> which belongs to the <a href="/wiki/A.P._Moller-Maersk_Group" title="A.P. Moller-Maersk Group" class="mw-redirect">A.P. Moller-Maersk Group</a>. Typical bore size for low-speed engines ranges from approximately 35 to 98 cm (14 to 39 in). As of 2008, all produced low-speed engines with <a href="/wiki/Crosshead_bearing" title="Crosshead bearing" class="mw-redirect">crosshead bearings</a> are in-line configurations; no Vee versions have been produced.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Supercharging_and_turbocharging">Supercharging and turbocharging</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Supercharging and turbocharging">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Supercharging and turbocharging" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Most diesels are now <a href="/wiki/Turbocharging" title="Turbocharging" class="mw-redirect">turbocharged</a> and some are both turbo charged and <a href="/wiki/Supercharged" title="Supercharged" class="mw-redirect">supercharged</a>. Because diesels do not have fuel in the cylinder before combustion is initiated, more than one bar (100 kPa) of air can be loaded in the cylinder without preignition. A turbocharged engine can produce significantly more power than a naturally aspirated engine of the same configuration, as having more air in the cylinders allows more fuel to be burned and thus more power to be produced. A supercharger is powered mechanically by the engine's <a href="/wiki/Crankshaft" title="Crankshaft">crankshaft</a>, while a turbocharger is powered by the engine exhaust, not requiring any mechanical power. Turbocharging can improve the fuel economy<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> of diesel engines by recovering waste heat from the exhaust, increasing the excess air factor, and increasing the ratio of engine output to friction losses.</p>
<p><span id="Scavenge_blower"></span>A <a href="/wiki/Two-stroke_engine" title="Two-stroke engine">two-stroke engine</a> does not have a discrete exhaust and intake stroke and thus is incapable of self-aspiration. Therefore all two-stroke engines must be fitted with a <a href="/wiki/Scavenge_blower" title="Scavenge blower" class="mw-redirect">blower</a> to charge the cylinders with air and assist in dispersing exhaust gases, a process referred to as <a href="/wiki/Scavenging_(automotive)" title="Scavenging (automotive)">scavenging</a>. In some cases, the engine may also be fitted with a turbocharger, whose output is directed into the blower inlet. A few designs employ a hybrid turbocharger for scavenging and charging the cylinders, which device is mechanically driven at cranking and low speeds to act as a blower.</p>
<p>As turbocharged or supercharged engines produce more power for a given engine size as compared to naturally aspirated engines, attention must be paid to the mechanical design of components, lubrication, and cooling to handle the power. Pistons are usually cooled with lubrication oil sprayed on the bottom of the piston. Large engines may use water, sea water, or oil supplied through <a href="/wiki/Telescoping_(mechanics)" title="Telescoping (mechanics)">telescoping</a> pipes attached to the crosshead.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_and_future_developments">Current and future developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Current and future developments">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Current and future developments" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="rellink boilerplate seealso">See also: <a href="/wiki/Diesel_car_history" title="Diesel car history" class="mw-redirect">Diesel car history</a></div>
<p>As of 2008, many <a href="/wiki/Common_rail" title="Common rail">common rail</a> and unit injection systems already employ new injectors using stacked <a href="/wiki/Piezoelectric" title="Piezoelectric" class="mw-redirect">piezoelectric</a> wafers in lieu of a solenoid, giving finer control of the injection event.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Variable_geometry_turbocharger" title="Variable geometry turbocharger" class="mw-redirect">Variable geometry turbochargers</a> have flexible vanes, which move and let more air into the engine depending on load. This technology increases both performance and fuel economy. Boost lag is reduced as turbo impeller inertia is compensated for.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Accelerometer pilot control (APC) uses an <a href="/wiki/Accelerometer" title="Accelerometer">accelerometer</a> to provide feedback on the engine's level of noise and vibration and thus instruct the ECU to inject the minimum amount of fuel that will produce quiet combustion and still provide the required power (especially while idling).<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The next generation of common rail diesels is expected to use variable injection geometry, which allows the amount of fuel injected to be varied over a wider range, and variable valve timing (see Mitsubishi's <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_4N1_engine" title="Mitsubishi 4N1 engine">4N13</a> diesel engine) similar to that on <a href="/wiki/Petrol_engine" title="Petrol engine">petrol engines</a>. Particularly in the United States, coming tougher emissions regulations present a considerable challenge to diesel engine manufacturers. Ford's <a href="/w/index.php?title=HyTrans_Project&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="HyTrans Project (page does not exist)">HyTrans Project</a> has developed a system which starts the ignition in 400 ms, saving a significant amount of fuel on city routes, and there are other methods to achieve even more efficient combustion, such as <a href="/wiki/HCCI" title="HCCI" class="mw-redirect">homogeneous charge compression ignition</a>, being studied.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Japanese and Swedish vehicle manufacturers are also developing diesel engines that run on dimethyl ether (DME).<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Some recent diesel engine models utilize a <a href="/wiki/Copper_in_heat_exchangers" title="Copper in heat exchangers">copper alloy heat exchanger</a> technology (<a href="/wiki/CuproBraze" title="CuproBraze">CuproBraze</a>) to take advantage of benefits in terms of thermal performance, heat transfer efficiency, strength/durability, corrosion resistance, and reduced emissions from higher operating temperatures.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Notes">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Notes" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-45">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the 16-cylinder variant of EMD's 645F series, a Roots-blown engine could produce a maximum of 2,000 horsepower (1,500&#160;kW). A turbocharged engine could produce up to 3,500 horsepower (2,600&#160;kW)—a 75% increase—although the engine was not particularly reliable at this rating; however a a 50% increase to 3,000 horsepower (2,200&#160;kW) proved to be exceptionally reliable and most such examples are still operating today, some forty years after these were built.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<li id="cite_note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Incredible as it may seem, the archetype high-speed two-stroke diesel engine had, in 1938, a per-cylinder displacement of 71 cubic inches, yet by 1985, less than fifty years later, the medium-speed diesel engine had a per cylinder displacement which was <i>ten times</i> as large ... 710 cubic inches per cylinder.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">standard p-v diagrams.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-47">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><span class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.bath.ac.uk/~ccsshb/12cyl/">"The Most Powerful Diesel Engine in the World"</a>. Bath.ac.uk. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.webcitation.org/5uL1LF86x">Archived</a> from the original on November 18, 2010<span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Charge air induction is necessarily symmetrical about bottom dead center; in the Electro-Motive Diesel examples, this is 45 degrees before bottom dead center to 45 degrees after bottom dead center; the only remaining variables are the timing of the opening and closing of the four poppet valves in the head, and these are timed to maximize both scavenging and compression.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Electro-Motive Diesel, a medium-speed engine, initiates injection at 4 degrees before top dead center; a high-speed engine may initiate injection at another point in the cycle.</span></li>
